It is well known that nanostructured polyaniline has a large accessible surface area and a low electrical resistance, making it a particularly suitable catalyst support for promoting the catalytic ability of Pt particles. A series of micro/nanostructured polyanilines were synthesized and their morphology dependent electrochemical properties for acting as a catalyst support for DMFC applications were investigated.154 These micro/nanostructures included submicron spheres, hollow microspheres, nanotubes, and nanofibers. Among the four micro/nanostructures, polyaniline nanofibers (PANFs) demonstrated their superiority with a high electrochemically active surface area. Compared with Pt/Vulcan, the Pt/PANF electrode possessed higher electrocatalytic activity and exhibited double power density. Moreover, Pt/PANF was superior to Pt/Vulcan in the aspect of operation stability based on a continuous discharge for 5 h.
The DMFC test results indicated that the MEA prepared by employing the polyaniline (PANI) modified anode made by electro-polymerization of aniline and trifluoromethane sulfonic acid (TFMSA) under galvanostatic conditions exhibited higher performance compared to the conventional MEA in terms of maximum power density (105 and 75 mW cm−2, respectively) and lower methanol crossover caused by PANI as a barrier in the modified anode.155
A facile methodology for improvement in the stability of the PtRu/CB electrocatalyst is described in the literature,156 in which PtRu alloyed nanoparticles are decorated with nitrogen-doped carbon (NxC) originating from the carburization of in situ polymerized polyaniline (PANI) as shown in Scheme 4. Fuel cell tests demonstrated that the NxC-protected PtRu/CB@NxC electrocatalyst showed performance comparable to that of the commercial PtRu/CB catalyst and increased stability (the loss of active sites after stability estimation was 10% and 50% for the PtRu/CB@NxC and PtRu/CB, respectively).
 Scheme 4  Synthetic route of the PtRu/CB@NxC electrocatalyst (APS: ammonium persulfate). Reproduced with permission from ref. 156, Copyright 2018 Elsevier Publications. 
Poly(2,5-benzimidazole) (ABPBI) is one of the most promising conductive polymers in high-temperature FCs because it can be prepared from inexpensive and commercially available single monomers and also possesses high thermal and mechanical stabilities.157 New PtRu electrocatalysts supported on carbon black (CB), in which the PtRu nanoparticles were structurally sandwiched by poly(vinylpyrrolidone) (PVP) and ABPBI, were prepared.158 Due to the first PVP coating on CB, the micropores on CB are capped resulting in high Pt utilization efficiency (94%) and catalytic activity toward methanol oxidation; the second coating (ABPBI) on PtRu nanoparticles fabricated via in situ polymerization improves the PtRu stability. The fuel cell performance of the double polymer coated electrocatalyst reaches 103 mW cm−2, which is among the highest values reported in the literature.
Increased stability and enhanced CO tolerance was demonstrated for an electrocatalyst composed of Pt deposited on carbon nanotubes wrapped in poly[2,2′-(2,6-pyridine)-5,5′-bibenzimidazole] (PyPBI) further covered by poly(vinylpyrrolidone) (PVP) by “a double-polymer coating method” (see Scheme 5).159 The PVP-coated electrocatalyst showed an enhancement in the Pt stability because of the stabilization of the Pt-NPs by the PyPBI and PVP. After the durability test, the PVP-coated electrocatalyst still showed ∼8 times higher CO tolerance because of the presence of the PVP bound stably to PyPBI.
 Scheme 5  Schematic illustration of the preparation of the MWNTs/PyPBI/Pt/PVP together with the chemical structures of PyPBI and PVP. Reproduced with permission from ref. 159, Copyright 2016 Elsevier Publications. 
It should be noted that the number of studies on conducting polymers as support materials for Pt-based catalysts is not as large as the number of studies carried out using various carbonaceous support materials, and in the future, more in-depth and extensive research is needed on these catalytic systems to achieve better DMFC performance.160
Three-dimensional (3D) carbon-based materials including porous carbon, carbon nanotube networks and graphene aggregates have been suggested as suitable catalyst supports, based on the consideration that their large porous structures and high conductivity could benefit the mass transport and electrochemical oxidation of methanol fuels. A 3D N-doped graphene aerogel (N-GA) with porous structures and uniformly distributed PtRu NPs (N-GA/PtRu) exhibited unprecedented activity, excellent tolerance and good stability towards the methanol electrochemical oxidation reaction.161
Notably, N-GA/PtRu can be directly used as the anode of DMFCs by simple physical pressing without the need for any binders or additives. The DMFCs constructed with N-GA/PtRu display remarkably large power density and current density under the working conditions. These 3D graphene-based nanocomposites, with easy synthesis, simple manufacturing process and high performance, hold great promise for the replacement of conventional carbon-based hybrids in practical fuel cell applications.
In a hierarchical N-doped carbon nanotube-graphene hybrid nanostructure (NCNT-GHN) used as an efficient support for PtRu nanoparticles, the simultaneous utilization of both the external and internal space of the NCNTs would be possible as the catalyst particles would also access the graphene layers via the open-ended tips, as shown in Scheme 6.162
 Scheme 6  Structural illustration of NCNT-GHN with open-ended tips. Reproduced with permission from ref. 162, Copyright 2011 Wiley Publications. 
The graphene layers inside the inner cavity of the CNTs can afford additional anchoring points for metal catalyst nanoparticles in virtue of the open-ended tips of CNTs. Moreover, the graphene layers can serve as additional electron transport pathways inside the CNTs, which is beneficial to the formation of an effective three-dimensional conductive network between different NCNT-GHNs. Improved single-cell performances with long-term stability compared to conventional CNTs and commercial catalysts were achieved due to the synergistic effect of the hierarchical structure (graphene–CNT hybrid) and electronic modulation (N-doping).
Due to its extreme chemical and electrochemical stability, diamond is considered one of the most promising alternative support materials, although it has insufficient electrical conductivity in its undoped form.164 However, coating insulating diamond powder with a thin boron-doped layer resulted in an increase of the conductivity from near zero (<10−5 S cm−1) to 1.5 S cm−1.
It has been demonstrated that Pt and Ru nanoparticles deposited on undoped and boron doped diamond nanoparticles (BDDNP), which were prepared using NaBH4 as a reducing agent and sodium dodecyl benzene sulfonate (SDBS) as a surfactant, can be successfully applied as electrocatalysts in DMFCs and hydrogen-fuelled PEM fuel cells.163
In ref. 165, the development of boron and nitrogen co-doped carbon nanotubes (BNC NTs) as a support for Pt was demonstrated. Half-cell results demonstrated that boron doping is the key step to improve the catalytic activities and CO-resistance ability due to the combination effects involving firm B–C and N–C bonds, the stronger electron transfer in the nanotube structure among Pt, B and N, and the stronger adsorption intensity of oxygen species from doped B.


3.3.3 Effect of the oxophilic metal-containing support. 
Numerous investigations suggest that some of the CO-poisoning-related issues of the Pt/C electrocatalysts can be overcome by applying a second oxophilic surface metal, which facilitates easy oxidation of the CO contaminant via the so-called bifunctional mechanism, provided an intimate contact between the oxophilic sites and Pt is ensured.115–117 Classical examples include Pt-based alloys or Pt on an oxophilic metal-containing support. Some examples of the effect of the presence of oxophilic metal-containing supports on the DMFC performance of the related Pt electrocatalysts are presented in Table 6. It should be noted that Table 6 includes the most basic examples demonstrating the various possible forms of adding an oxophilic metal to support materials: (i) mixing the appropriate oxide and carbon powder, (ii) preparing oxide/mixed oxide–carbon composites, or (iii) using highly conductive nanostructured oxophilic metal-containing nitride compounds.

179





Metal oxides based on non-noble metals have promising characteristics for their application in fuel cells. For example, the most successful alternative support material, TiO2, which is non-toxic, has high mechanical, chemical and redox stability, satisfies the stability requirements and its interaction with Pt is also beneficial.166–168
Salgado et al.169 investigated the performance of PtRu electrocatalysts supported by different carbon materials containing HNO3-functionalized carbon black, a mesoporous carbon (CMK-3), a physical mixture of (TiO2 + C) and a reference carbon thermally treated in a helium atmosphere. It has been demonstrated that the highest power density was obtained on the PtRu/C–HNO3 electrode, revealing that the presence of oxygenated groups on carbon materials had an obvious positive effect on the methanol oxidation reaction.
The effect of introducing 5, 15, and 25 wt% of commercial and in-house prepared titania (TiO2) into the Pt–Ru/C anode electrocatalyst on the performance of DMFCs was investigated in ref. 170. The highest power densities were found to be 70.5 mW cm−2 and 70.9 mW cm−2 at 80 °C and 1 M methanol for the DMFCs based on Pt–Ru/C–TiO2 anode electrocatalysts containing 5 wt% of commercial and in-house developed TiO2, respectively.
This performance is about 27.9% higher than that of the DMFC based on the commercial Pt–Ru/C anode electrocatalyst. The results of the 4 h short-term stability tests showed that introduction of 5 wt% commercial TiO2 into the commercial Pt–Ru/C anode electrocatalyst improved its stability characteristics significantly.
It has been demonstrated that the methanol oxidation reaction on Pt particles can be promoted by a TiO2 support, which can lower the adsorption energy of CO.171 Moreover, TiO2 nanoparticles provide additional OH species and these OH species facilitate the conversion of CO derivative intermediates into CO2.
A novel class of electrocatalysts comprises Pt catalysts supported on titanium dioxide doped with certain transition metals (e.g., W, Mo, Ta and Sn).180–184 These mixed oxide-containing catalytic systems demonstrated increased activity and enhanced CO tolerance, but the electronic conductivities of these materials are still substantially lower than those of carbon catalyst supports. Moreover, even if the synthesis of the alternative electrocatalysts is feasible in laboratory quantities, the upscaling of the preparation is often difficult.
Compared to Pt/TiO2–C and commercial Pt/C catalysts, the best performing Pt/Ti0.9Sn0.1O2–C catalyst exhibited the highest activity, evidenced by methanol oxidation and CO stripping experiments.172
The enhanced activity for Pt supported on Ti0.9Sn0.1O2–C is due to the high content of the OH group on Ti0.9Sn0.1O2 along with the strengthened metal—support interactions. Both promote the oxidation of the poisoning CO adsorbed on Pt active sites.
Single fuel cell tests show that the maximum power density of the direct methanol fuel cell using Pt/graphene–TiO2 as the anode catalyst is increased by 55% compared with that using Pt/graphene catalyst under the same operating conditions (see Fig. 12).173 The significantly enhanced electrochemical performance was ascribed to (i) the synergetic effect between Pt, graphene and TiO2 as well as (ii) strong metal–support interaction (SMSI) between Pt nanoparticles and TiO2.
 Fig. 12  Facile one-pot synthesis of the Pt/graphene–TiO2 hybrid catalyst with enhanced methanol electro-oxidation performance: HRTEM images of the Pt/graphene–TiO2 catalyst (left) and performance of single DMFCs with different catalysts (right). Reproduced with permission from ref. 173, Copyright 2015 Elsevier Publications. 
Undoubtedly, the design of a new catalyst support with properties such as high density, high electrical conductivity, and high stability/durability in acidic media, which provide an accessible and sufficient surface area for good dispersion and nucleation of fine metal nanoparticles, is very important for achieving the goals of creating a high performance anodic electrocatalyst with reduced precious metal content for DMFCs.
Titanium nitride (TiN) has higher electrical conductivity (4000 S cm−1) than carbon (1190 S cm−1) and higher density (5.1 g cm−3) than carbon (1.9 g cm−3), which results in a thin layer of the catalyst supported on TiN in contrast with carbon thus offering low mass transfer limitations.185 Taking this into account, the study of highly conductive TiN with a high specific surface area as a support for Pt (Ru) is one of the promising directions for creating a new generation support.
High surface area (∼125 m2 g−1) of the CSG-Pt(Ru)/TiN materials prepared using the complexed sol–gel (CSG) was achieved by the controlled removal of carbon from Pt(Ru)/TiN/C materials using an oxidizing atmosphere (1% O2–Ar) and low temperature (200 °C).174 Single cell DMFC performance showed 56% improved maximum power density and superior electrochemical stability for the Pt50(Ru50)/TiN catalyst, compared to that of the commercial JM-Pt(Ru) (Johnson Matthey) catalyst. This behaviour was attributed to the uniform dispersion of Pt(Ru) achieved on a highly conductive nanostructured TiN support yielding higher electrochemical active surface area and lower charge transfer resistance than commercial JM-Pt(Ru).
Creating more intimate contact between the TiO2 nanoparticles and highly conductive carbon is a key issue to compensate for the low electrical conductivity of TiO2. TiO2-embedded carbon nanofibers, TECNFs, meet these requirements and can be a promising candidate for use as a catalyst support. The power generation performance of the 20 wt% Pt–Ru (with a Pt:Ru atomic ratio of 1:1) supported on TECNF (PtRu/TECNF) was compared to that of commercial PtRu (53 wt%)/Ccom.175 The nanofiber support was prepared by the electrospinning of polyacrylonitrile (PAN) with TiO2 nanoparticles followed by carbonization and steam activation of the nanofiber. The ultrahigh catalytic activity was demonstrated by the two-times higher DMFC power output using PtRu/TECNF, in spite of quarter the PtRu loading on the electrode, compared to that using the commercial PtRu/C. In ref. 176 the power density was maximized using 30 wt% PtRu/TECNF, which showed 173 mW cm−2 at 80 °C that was 26% higher than that of commercial PtRu/C. The ultrahigh catalytic activity of PtRu/TECNF is attributed to the metal–support interaction, which efficiently occurs at the PtRu/TECNF structure.
Among metal oxide supports, CeO2 is one of the most attractive materials that have been thoroughly studied as catalysts and as structural and electronic promoters of heterogeneous catalytic reactions.186 The improved activity was sometimes interpreted via the bifunctional mechanism and the high oxygen storage capacity of CeO2.187 A series of CeO2 embedded carbon nanofibers, CECNFs, with different CeO2 contents was used as a support for PtRu nanoparticles.177 A DMFC with the PtRu/CECNF exhibited more than 2.5 times high power density with one half the PtRu loading compared to that of the commercial PtRu/Ccom catalyst. The increased activity was attributed to the strong interaction between the metal and oxide in the embedded nanofiber structure.
IrO2 exhibits excellent corrosion resistance in acidic medium, good electrical conductivity and high electrocatalytic activity toward oxygen evolution.188 The results presented in ref. 178 evidenced that a multifunctional catalyst composed of a mixture of in-house prepared high metal surface area 50 wt% PtRu/C and IrO2 nanopowders operated better than bare PtRu/C for methanol oxidation since this multi-step process requires different functionalities to speed up the reaction rate. To address the need for reduction of noble metal amount for cost-effective DMFCs, a Pt loading as low as 0.5 mg cm−2 in the anode was used in all experiments.
In ref. 179, a new family of materials for electrodes was presented: mesoporous carbon ceramics (MCCs) were obtained by the addition of commercial graphite to the SBA-15 mesoporous silica synthesis gel with SiO2/C weight ratios of 1/1 and 1/3. When used as the anode, the MCC-SBA-15 (SiO2/C = 1/3) composite supported PtRu catalyst showed performance comparable to PtRu/Vulcan XC-72R.
In conclusion, materials composed of Pt NPs, oxides/mixed oxides (TiO2, CeO2, IrO2, etc.) and carbon materials with large surface area are identified as efficient electrocatalysts since they combine the good conductivity of carbon and corrosion resistance of the oxide with the synergistic effect between metal oxides and Pt.


3.3.4 Pt-based alloy-type electrocatalysts. 
The influence of the Pt binary, ternary, and quaternary metal alloys supported on different carbonaceous materials on the active DMFC performances is presented in Table 7.

In a novel class of electrocatalysts the additive often presents as an alloying component in the carbon supported Pt nanoparticles; examples include Pt–Sn,189–191 Pt–Co,192 Pt–Fe,193 Pt–Pd,194 or Pt–Ru,195,196 among which Pt–Ru is commercially available as the state-of-the-art CO-tolerant electrocatalyst recommended for DMFCs. It has been demonstrated by Antolini et al.197 that among various bimetallic catalysts, PtCo can be considered better for methanol oxidation under acidic or alkaline conditions as Co presents a higher degree of alloying with Pt compared to other transition metals. Recently, the utilization of catalyst-coated membrane (CCM) and spraying methods has been investigated in the fabrication of MEAs for DMFCs using Pt–Co/C catalysts with different Pt/Co ratios.198 With the spray method, the best MEA performance was achieved with a Pt/Co ratio of 60/40 and with the CCM method with a Pt/Co ratio of 40/60, but the spray method gave better performance.
PtCo alloy based highly efficient nanoelectrocatalysts on a reduced graphene oxide (rGO) matrix have been synthesized for the electrooxidation of methanol, by the chemical reduction method.199 The peak power density of 118.4 mW cm−2 was obtained for the PtCo (1:9)/rGO catalyst in a DMFC at 100 °C, 1 bar, and 2 M methanol as the anode feed, which is ∼3 times higher than that of the Pt/C catalyst.
As shown in Scheme 7 the construction of the reduced graphene oxide rGO/PANI/Pt–Pd composite involves two steps comprising the synthesis of polyaniline (PANI) on GO and in situ reduction of GO and metal precursors.200 The cumulative features of rGO/PANI/Pt–Pd include an active carbon support and an extended architecture of electron conducting channels, and the abundant methanol oxidation centers offer an excellent DMFC power density of 117.45 mW cm−2 and cell durability over 70 h.
 Scheme 7  Schematic illustration of (i) the preparation of rGO/PANI/Pt–Pd and (ii) the electrocatalytic oxidation of methanol on rGO/PANI/Pt–Pd. Reproduced with permission from ref. 200, Copyright 2019 Elsevier Publications. 
Enhancement of the durability of the composite is provided by the strong interaction between PANI and Pt–Pd through a nitride bond (see Scheme 7).
Lee et al. reported the synthesis of 1.7 nm highly stable and dispersed carbon supported and unsupported bimetallic PtRu, PtMo, PtW and PtNi alloys, which were obtained using LiBH4 in THF as a hydrogen source without the addition of a stabilizing agent.201 The importance of this early study to this day is that it summarizes the basic requirements of catalysts for the enhancement of the DMFC performance. Nanoparticles for fuel cell applications require several characteristics: (i) alloy formation and proper surface chemical composition (metallic Pt and oxidation states of the second metal) to satisfy the bifunctional effect and electronic effect; (ii) controlled surface structure, preferably a highly active and thermodynamically stable surface (111) for a structural effect; (iii) smaller size for preferred single-crystal faces and lower Pt metal loading; (iv) lower onset potential for methanol oxidation to be used for higher electrocatalytic activity. Thus, the high activity of these electrocatalysts was rationalized by alloy formation, surface structure and chemical composition of the nanoparticles.
The preparation of PtRuRhNi multimetallic nanoparticles by the borohydride reduction method for enhanced methanol electrooxidation in a DMFC was described in ref. 202. The catalytic activity of the PtRuRhNi alloy electrocatalyst was superior to that of PtRu, from the standpoint of its high oxidation current, power density, and better stability in the electrochemical half- and single-cell measurements.
The influence of thermal treatment in different environments of the PtRuMo/C catalyst has been investigated for CO and methanol electrooxidation in a half cell and in a DMFC single cell.203 The electrochemical characterization revealed that although all ternary catalysts were more active towards CO and methanol oxidation than the binary catalyst, the catalyst treated with H2 shows improved performance by ca. 15% with respect to the ternary catalysts treated either in helium or without helium treatment. The synergistic effect of the three metals seems to be improved and the Mo–Pt and Mo–Ru interaction strengthened.
Chen et al.204 demonstrated that the performance of the DMFC using PtRuMo/CNTs as the anode catalyst is better than that of PtRu/CNTs and PtRu/C catalysts due to the electronic effect, bifunctional mechanism and hydrogen transfer.
Since the adsorption of hydrogen on the phosphide materials might be beneficial for dehydrogenation during the electro-oxidation of methanol, the performance of a nanostructured PtRu catalyst for methanol oxidation could be significantly improved using the CoP material.205 A maximum power density of 85.7 mW cm−2 achieved at 30 °C on the PtRu–CoP/C catalyst is much higher than that of state-of-the-art commercial PtRu/C catalyst at 70 °C (63.1 mW cm−2). The outstanding catalytic activity and stability observed on the PtRu–CoP/C catalyst should be attributed to the synergistic effect between the nanostructured PtRu and CoP, in which the presence of CoP could largely slow down the loss of Ru and Pt in the PtRu catalyst system and anti-CO poisoning ability.
Recently, a molybdenum phosphide/multiwalled CNT (MoP/MWCNT) catalyst support has been synthesized206 in which fine and well-crystallized MoP nanocrystals were deposited on the sidewalls of the MWCNTs. Electrochemical tests showed that the novel Pt–MoP/MWCNT catalyst demonstrated a good catalytic activity of 1063 mA mg−1 Pt during the methanol electrooxidation reaction, which is 1.6 times higher than that reported for PtRu–CoP/C (670 mA mg−1 Pt).
N-doped graphene (NG) supported ternary PtRuFe nanocatalysts with size between 2 and 3 nm were synthesized using the microwave-assisted polyol method as shown in Scheme 8.207
 Scheme 8  Schematic diagram for the synthesis of PtRuFe/NG. Reproduced with permission from ref. 207, Copyright 2017 Elsevier Publications. 
The maximum power densities delivered by PEMFCs and DMFCs using N-doped graphene (NG) supported ternary PtRuFe nanocatalysts were 870 and 112 mW cm−2, respectively, which are 25% higher than that of the undoped PtRuFe/rGO counterpart. Also, PtRuFe/NG showed 2- to 3-fold improvement in PEMFC and DMFC performances as compared with previously reported values for mono-, bi-, and tri-metallic catalysts supported on NG and other carbon substrates. The enhanced electrochemical activity may be attributed to the synergetic effect of nitrogen-doping along with the bifunctional and ligand effects of alloying low-cost Fe with Pt and Ru.
It should be noted that the bifunctional ternary PtRuFe nanocatalysts supported on N-doped graphene have excellent potential to catalyse both MOR and ORR with remarkable reduction in the processing cost of fuel cells which is the primary problem encountered in the commercialization of fuel cells.
In ref. 208, the performances of the DMFCs assembled with Pt–NiTiO3/C and Pt–Ru–NiTiO3/C anode electrocatalyst materials were investigated. The overall results suggest that the presence of the NiTiO3/C support material increases DMFC performances when Ru is combined with Pt even in the presence of lower Pt loading (0.5 mgPt cm−2). The increased catalytic activity of the anode electrocatalyst shows the mixed synergistic effect of Ru and NiTiO3 and it helps to remove the COads from the catalytic sites. Thus, the nickel titanate–carbon composite can be considered as a promising support material for achieving improved performance for low cost and low power density DMFC applications.
A quaternary catalyst with the composition Pt34Ru30Ir13Ni23, developed using a robotic dispenser and combinatorial optical screening method, was the most active in comparison with the commercial Pt50–Ru50 binary catalyst.209 The effect of the carbon support on methanol oxidation was also investigated using hollow mesoporous carbon (HMC), mesoporous CMK-3, and commercial Vulcan XC-72 (VC) as supporting materials. The Pt34Ru30Ir13Ni23/HMC catalyst had the highest activity, which was attributed to (i) the active quaternary catalyst composition, the activity of which can be explained by a bifunctional mechanism, an electronic effect, and a stability effect arising from the addition of Ni and Ir; and (ii) a unique hierarchical nanostructured carbon support with a large surface area and pore volume, which contributes not only to the homogeneous dispersion of small catalyst nanoparticles, but also fast mass transport in the catalyst layer.
It should be noted that there are numerous reports on the development of Pt-based alloy-type electrocatalysts, which can be successfully used in DMFCs. Moreover, interest in this topic has increased significantly in recent years. Several examples of new catalyst systems that can be used as promising electrocatalysts in DMFCs are presented below.
The synthesis of wicker-like networked PtFe nanowires (NWs) presented in ref. 216 could be a new guideline to tune the structure and composition of nanocatalysts for their use in DMFCs and beyond. It was shown that composition-optimized Pt3Fe NWs had the highest catalytic activity and stability in the MOR and EOR, much better than Pt/C.
Bimetallic aerogels of networked Pt–Sn NWs have been successfully synthesized by Song et al.217 The optimized networked Pt6Sn3 NWs also exhibited enhanced stability toward both the EOR and MOR compared to Pt/C. The present investigations suggested that the networked NWs with high surface areas and rich defects are indeed a unique class of efficient electrocatalysts for alcohol electrooxidation.
The electrodeposition of Pt-based catalysts (PtPd, PtRu, and PtSn) using the pulse current deposition technique was carried out on a titanium substrate to prepare electrodes of different compositions to identify a possible catalyst offering high catalytic activity towards the MOR.218 Among the various compositions and binary metals studied, Pt50Ru50/Ti showed the highest activity and stability.
A general strategy for the synthesis of PtM (M = Fe, Co, Ni) decorated three-dimensional (3D) hollow graphene nanospheres for efficient methanol electrooxidation was demonstrated by Qiu et al.219 This study provides a versatile approach of realizing controlled synthesis of 3D graphene–metal hybrid nanostructures irrespective of the components of the metal domains, and will pave the way for the design of hetero-nanostructures with optimized morphologies and functions.
Recently, structurally ordered Pt-based intermetallic NPs have been used to further improve the electrochemical performance of alloy-type catalysts. Thus, the structurally ordered PtSn NPs supported on Sb-doped SnO2 (ATO) demonstrated remarkably enhanced MOR activity and durability in comparison with the disordered PtSn alloy and commercial Pt/C.220 It has been demonstrated that ATO not only acts as a conductive support for Pt NP deposition and formation ofa strong metal–support interaction, but also provides the Sn source for the transformation of Pt NPs into the structurally ordered PtSn intermetallic NPs.
Pt-based alloy catalysts, Pt–Ni/pCNFs and Pt–Ag–Ni/pCNFs (pCNFs: porous-structured nanofibers nanocomposites), were successfully prepared by electrospinning, impregnation, carbonization and replacement reaction.221 Taking advantages of the three-component alloy system and porous structure of nanofibers, the Pt–Ag–Ni/pCNFs catalyst demonstrated superior electrocatalytic activity for methanol oxidation, tolerance to CO and stability.

3.4 Comparison of active DMFC performance with different cathode electrocatalysts
The influence of different cathode electrocatalysts on the active DMFC performance is presented in Table 8.

Various types of carbonaceous materials such as carbon black, multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWNTs) and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) have been used as supports for cathode Pt catalysts.210 Pt/C and Pt/MWNTs showed high and quite comparable power densities (74.0 and 74.2 mW cm−2, respectively), but long-term testing of DMFCs indicated that superior stability was observed only on the Pt/MWNT catalyst.
A mesoporous carbon-supported Pt catalyst with high specific surface area (600 m2 g−1) was used as a cathode catalyst in DMFCs, where a 30% increase in power density compared to Pt/Vulcan was obtained under the same conditions.211 In this study mesoporous carbon was obtained by the carbonization of a structured resorcinol formaldehyde polymer, using a cationic polyelectrolyte as a structuring agent.
The influence of active screen plasma (ASP) treatment temperature and duration on the growth of the Pt nanowire catalyst layer was systemically studied.212 Results revealed that the ASP treatment done at 120 °C for 10 min can effectively promote the growth of Pt nanowires on carbon paper gas diffusion layer, which exhibited the best catalysis performance as compared with the untreated gas diffusion layer (GDL).
Pd-based electrodes (Pd/KB, Pd4Co1/KB and Pd10Co1/KB; KB: Ketjenblack) have shown to be highly appropriate for their use as cathode catalysts in DMFCs in terms of reduction of costs and increase of energy density of the device, since they are much more tolerant to permeated methanol than the benchmark Pt/C catalyst.213 In fact, the cell based on the Pd/KB catalyst provided a higher performance than that based on Pt/C at high methanol concentration (>5 M). The durability tests highlighted the advantage of including a small amount of cobalt in the crystalline structure of Pd, in terms of stability, pointing to a powerful candidate cathode material for DMFCs.
In ref. 214, Pt–C core–shell catalysts supported on CNFs were synthesized and used as cathodes for DMFCs. The synthesized catalysts exhibited higher methanol tolerance than conventional Pt catalysts because the carbon shell effectively protected the Pt core from methanol by selectively permeating oxygen. The selective permeability of the carbon shell increased with the degree of graphitization. The Pt@CS/CNF catalyst heat treated in nitrogen at 900 °C showed the highest methanol tolerance and performance.
A facile one pot aqueous method for support-free Pd85Pt15 nano-porous structures (NPoS) synthesised from PdMn nano-alloys under ambient conditions was recently reported.215 It has been demonstrated that a complete carbon support-free MEA prepared using a support-free Pd85Pt15 NPoS cathode material and Pt NP anode with consistently reduced catalyst layer thickness showed a very high DMFC activity attributable to the zero carbon environment, enhanced catalyst utilization and mass transport on both sides.
3.5 Direct methanol fuel cell with platinum-free electrodes
As a result of the environmental problems caused by the use of fossil fuels, many scientists worldwide are focused on finding some other efficient alternatives. Thus, although high hopes have been placed on hydrogen fuel cells, we face huge challenges related to the transport, storage and use of pure hydrogen. Indeed, the huge cost involved makes this process difficult with the current technologies. Alternatively, methanol (CH3OH) exhibits very attractive advantages: it does not require costly and critical conditions for storage, shows a higher energy density and, obviously, is easier and safer to transport. Therefore, a transition to a methanol-based energy and related economy seems a more realistic goal in short and medium terms.
Nevertheless, it is worth mentioning that producing electricity from methanol at room temperature using the so-called DMFCs faces some obstacles. Thus, one of the main problems with DMFCs is the undesired methanol oxidation reaction, which occurs during methanol crossover, that is, when it passes from the anode to the cathode. This reaction leads to the degradation of the platinum (Pt) or other types of catalysts that are essential for the cell's operation. Obviously, many strategies were proposed in order to mitigate this problem in an attempt to overcome the cost and stability issues.

3.5.1 Methanol oxidation reaction (MOR) on Pt-free catalysts. 
As a very good non-platinic metal, Ni shows good electrocatalytic activity towards methanol.222 Moreover, Ni as a catalyst exhibits very important qualities: high stability, low cost and its oxides can easily oxidize the product formed during the MOR. Some other advantages should be added: nickel can be used as an anode catalyst in various forms such as alloys with other transition metals, complexes, nanoparticles, etc.223,224 In line, as a good electrode material it shows high surface area for enhancing the electrode kinetics by reducing the activation over potential.
The improvement of the methanol oxidation reaction on the anodic side of the DMFCs was related to the use of different metal oxides208,225–229 as support materials (e.g. SnO2, TiO2, CeO2, WO3, Co3V2O8/Co2V2O7, NiTiO3). Li et al.227 decorated NiO hollow spheres composed of stacked nanosheets with CeO2 nanoparticles (Ce molar amount of 1.5%, 2.5%, 10% and 15%) by a solvothermal synthesis route for the methanol reaction in alkaline medium. They found a proper loading content in the range of 2.5–5 mol%, which exhibits a high CeO2 dispersion on the NiO nanosheet surface. The morphology of the anode is presented in Fig. 13. The decoration process boosted the formation of the active NiOOH species. On the other hand, the mixture of the cerium valence states Ce3+/Ce4+ proven by XPS with more Ce3+ percentage, able to create a large number of surface oxygen vacancies, plays a good promoting role as a co-catalyst and greatly improves the electrocatalytic activity and stability. These inexpensive anode catalysts are good candidates for DMFC applications.
 Fig. 13  SEM images of 5CeO2–NiO composites (a–d) and EDS mapping images of 5CeO2–NiO composite spheres (f–h) taken from (e). Reproduced with permission from ref. 227, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications. 
Sunitha et al.230 performed a comparative study of non-noble metal catalysts for the methanol oxidation reaction by developing a Ni coated nickel mesh electrode working in acidic and alkaline medium.
The authors obtained a maximum current density of 0.025 and 0.030 A cm−2 in both environments but in the acidic medium the performance was achieved with a lower oxidation potential around 0.14 compared with 0.4 V in the alkaline medium. An improvement of the methanol oxidation was observed in the acidic medium due to the continuous purging of nitrogen. However, in the alkaline medium the methanol was oxidized with a higher rate of reaction due to the availability of oxygenated species which led to the easy adsorption of methanol.
Javan et al.231 assessed the electrocatalytic activity toward methanol oxidation by using reduced carbon quantum dots (RCQD) decorated with Ni nanoparticles with high surface area synthesized by a low cost and green electrochemical method. They found that the strong interaction between Ni and CQDs, the confinement effect of carbon quantum dots able to assure a very good dispersion of Ni, as well as the high specific surface area favour superior electron transfer on the surface of the catalyst and, consequently the methanol is more quickly oxidized. A high dispersion of Ni is highly desired in order to improve the electrochemical performance of the non-noble metal electrocatalysts for the methanol oxidation reaction.
Anode based Ni/NiO anchored on MWCNTs232 with uniform dispersion of Ni exhibited electrochemical activity (peak current density of 15.94 mA cm−2 at a peak potential of 0.43 mV) comparable with that of commercial Pt–Ru/C by testing in a DMFC single-cell. It was highlighted that by MWCNT incorporation the reaction kinetics was improved. Thus, the charge transfer resistance is diminished by the MWCNT addition and the electrons are more rapidly transferred from the catalyst to methanol.
Pattanayak et al.233 prepared an anode composite based on Cu2O NPs supported on graphene oxide (GO), polypyrrole (PPy) and polypyrrole prepared by a borohydride reduction method. They assessed the anode performance in the DMFC using Pt/C as the cathode. The maximum current density of 155 mA cm−2 obtained at +0.2 V and power density of 31 mW cm−2 as well as the stability were higher than the commercial Pt–Ru/C catalyst. The excellent methanol oxidation activity was related to the Cu2O trapped into the PPy grafted GO as a support matrix which enhanced the number of electrocatalytic active paths for the efficient electron transfer.
Transition metal chalcogenides (TMCs) and transition metal oxides (TMOs) represent a promising material group for methanol oxidation taking into account the low cost and electrochemical activity. Fe3O4@MoS2/rGO,234 (Co–FeNi)9S8 (ref. 235) and MoFeNiS TMC-based composite catalysts were designed for the MOR as well as for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER). The synergism between Co, Fe, and Ni, dispersion of (CoFeNi)9S8 nanoparticles on rGO, high specific surface area and porous structure of rGO are key factors in the methanol electrooxidation toward DMFCs.235 The replacement of Pt with Pd is desirable to overcome the major problems related to both high costs and the poisoning effects of Pt leading to the deactivation of the active sites and, consequently, to a dramatic drop of the electrochemical performance. The reasons for research directed toward designing catalysts based on Pd are: it is around fifty times more abundant than Pt, can achieve equivalent electrocatalytic activity and it is less susceptible to the formation of the reaction intermediates.
Sunil Kumar et al.236 investigated the methanol oxidation over composites based on Pd20Aux (x = 0, 5, 10 and 15) bimetallic nanoparticles embedded nitrogen doped graphene. They adopted a versatile hydrothermal polyol method, without any template involving the simultaneous reduction of Pd and Au in the presence of a solid support. Thus, they demonstrated that a high dispersion of metal nanoparticles, the strong electronic interactions with the support, the presence mainly of Pd0/Au0 as well as the pyrrolic nitrogen which contributes to the π system with one p electron are the key factors to design an efficient catalyst with enhanced reactivity and stability, CO tolerance and long-time durability for the MOR in DMFC applications.
Kang et al.237 developed an electrocatalyst based on hollow Rh nanostructures (Rh NSs) prepared by a hydrothermal synthesis route starting from Au-core@Rh-shell nanostructures (Au@Rh NSs). RhCl3·nH2O/gold(III) chloride hydrate (HAuCl4·3H2O) were used as inorganic precursors and diethylene glycol (DEG) as a surfactant in order to stabilize the colloid crystals in an attempt to grow specific surface facets. Aqua regia was used to remove the surfactant and Au. The electrooxidation tests of methanol, performed in 1 M KOH + 1 M CH3OH revealed that the Rh H-NSs possess a high inherent activity. One can notice that the catalyst exhibited a higher current density (12.4 A g−1) which is 3.5 times that of commercial Pt (3.5 A g−1) within 6000 s.
Pedireddy et al.238 reported the development of nanoporous gold with networks of interconnected ligaments and highly porous structure with activity for methanol electrooxidation and good carbon tolerance, evaluated over 4400 catalytic cycles. They underline their potential suitability for future industrial applications.
Liu et al.239 proved an enhancement of the methanol activity for Au NPs deposited on the graphene surface (Au-6T/SG/Cu) catalytic electrode. The electrochemical tests were performed under simulated solar light and monochromatic LED sources. They highlighted the beneficial role of the irradiation with the simulated solar light that led to the improvement in the electrooxidation performance. The obtained value of 288 μA μg−1 was approximately 1.7 times higher than that without irradiation.
In another study, Liu et al.240 designed Au caviar-like assemblies on an unheated glassy carbon substrate by using a home-built desktop DC ion-sputtering system. By this approach, it was found that the catalyst activity for the methanol electrooxidation reaction and the CO tolerance were significantly improved under simulated solar light irradiation. This favours the SPR-induced charge carrier separation as well as enhanced oxidation species on the Au surface.


3.5.2 Oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) on Pt-free catalysts. 
Martinaiou et al.,241 leading a comprehensive study, assessed the activity, stability and durability of Fe–N–C non-precious metal catalysts for the ORR in DMFCs. The electrocatalysts were prepared from polyaniline, dicyandiamide and iron acetate as precursors. They studied the effect of the presence of methanol and they observed that after load cycle durability tests the presence of methanol did not produce additional deactivation. For a deeper insight of the degradation phenomena, they studied the methanol tolerance, by applying a durability protocol in the presence and absence of methanol associated with a post-mortem Mössbauer spectroscopy.
Zhao et al.242 developed a 3D porous N-doped graphene aerogel (NGA) with high specific surface area (around 400 m2 g−1) by a hydrothermal synthesis route starting from graphene oxide (GO). The GO obtained via the modified Hummers' method243 was used as a building block and melamine-cyanurate supramolecule as both N source and self-sacrificial pore former. The presence of the different nitrogen species incorporated into the GO lattice such as pyridinic, graphitic, pyrrolic and oxidized nitrogen as well as the hierarchical pore rich structure gave rise to a good catalyst toward the ORR. The NGO electrochemical performance was comparable with the ORR catalytic activity of commercial Pt/C.
Lo Vecchio et al.244 obtained Co–N–C and Fe–N–C electrocatalysts following a chelating synthesis route assisted by ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (EDTA) as the nitrogen precursor and assessed their electrochemical performance by testing as cathodes in DMFCs. Taking into account the requirements related to the high electrical conductivity of the catalysts, the authors deposited the metal complexes on a high surface area oxidized carbon support and performed a final thermal treatment at 800–1000 °C in a nitrogen atmosphere. They assessed the nitrogen chemistry by XPS surface investigation and revealed that higher amounts of pyridinic-N species, with a strong interaction between N and metal atoms, resulted in better electrochemical performance. On the other hand, they reported a decline in the electrochemical activity due to the significant production of H2O2 in the ORR, when the pyrrolic-N species are formed on the surface. One can notice that only a small number of reliable tests on the durability/stability of DMFCs were performed. Indeed, in most DMFC related studies only the initial performance and/or methanol tolerance of non-precious metal cathode catalysts were tested.
The literature offers a few reports on the durability/stability of non-precious metal catalysts at the cathode of DMFCs.245–248 Starting from the standard durability test conditions, as defined by the US Department of Energy, the following degradation mechanisms were proposed for Fe–N–C catalysts. In the potential range assigned to load-cycle condition (0.6–1.0 V) (a) iron demetallation,249 (b) carbon corrosion,250 (c) protonation with subsequent anion binding,241 and (d) formation of reactive oxygen species (ROS) induced by hydrogen peroxide release are considered the most likely.
In a recent study published in ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces,214 a team of scientists led by Professor Oh Joong Kwon from Incheon National University, Korea reported a creative and effective solution. They fabricated—through a relatively simple procedure—a catalyst made of Pt nanoparticles encapsulated within a carbon shell. “The carbon shell acts as a molecular sieve and provides selectivity toward the desired reactants, which can actually reach the catalyst sites. This prevents the undesirable reaction of the Pt cores,” explains Professor Oh Joong Kwon. This method could be applied to other types of catalysts including non-Pt ones.
The way being open, the researchers found ways to keep under control the number of defects in the shell by simply changing the temperature during the heat treatment.
Following further experimental attempts and comparisons, this novel shelled catalyst outperformed commercial Pt catalysts and also offered much higher stability. Moreover, “DMFCs have a higher energy density than lithium-ion batteries and could therefore become alternative power sources for portable devices, such as laptops and smartphones,” Professor Oh Joong Kwon claims.
Goor et al.251 investigated MEAs, with anodes based on the Pt–Ru black catalyst and E-TEK cathode with 5 mgPt cm−2–20% PTFE carbon cloth substrate for mobility and portable applications. They found that the excellent DMFC performance – 181 mW cm−2 at 80 °C was related to the modification of the hydrophobicity of the BPP (Bi-Polar Plate) flow field channels. The main advantage of the DMFCs consists in quick charging with a low-cost liquid fuel that could be competitive with the hydrogen fuel cells.
Wang et al.252 incorporated an oxophilic Sn metal into the inexpensive Ag metal by a green electrodeposition method in order to obtain an Ag3Sn intermetallic catalyst with improved performance for the ORR. The presence of the metallic species on the surface was revealed by the XPS technique. They found that the electron transfer number (n) for the Ag3Sn catalyst was approximately 3.70, while for the commercial Pt/C it was approximately 4.0. These findings prove the formation of OOH– as an ORR intermediate. AgSn and Ag3Sn intermetallics253 obtained by combining electrochemical deposition and dealloying methods showed enhanced catalytic activity, stability after 5000 potential cycles and excellent alcohol tolerance. The ORR performance in alkaline media (half-wave potential of 0.810 mV and 0.790 mV) was comparable to that of commercial Pt/C (0.837 mV).
Wu et al.254 prepared Ag75M25 (M = Cu, Co, Fe, and In) and AgxCu100−x (x = 0, 25, 45, 50, 55, 75, 90, and 100) nanocatalysts and studied their electronic structures by valence band spectra investigation. They highlighted the effect of composition on the electrochemical properties in the ORR. The best results were obtained for Ag75Cu25 and Ag50Cu50 catalysts.
Zhang et al.255 investigated the reaction activation energy, reaction free energy and half-wave potential of AgCu alloys for the ORR using both theoretical and experimental methods, taking into account the crucial role of the highly active electrocatalysts in the commercialization of fuel cells. They found that a core–shell atom order should be designed for AgCu bimetallic nanoparticles accompanied by synergism in bimetallic catalysts to enhance their ORR activity.
Recently, Zeledón et al.256 studied the compositional effect of the bimetallic Ag1−xPdx (x = 0.1–0.9) thin film catalysts obtained by electron-beam physical vapour co-deposition on the intrinsic activity for the ORR. It was found that the Pd amount can be decreased by 50% without activity loss. By coupling the experimental and computational methods they concluded that the activity enhancements are due to the electronic improvement of the Pd–Pd active sites. Thus, the results revealed that materials with low precious-metal content which possess specific and tuneable properties can be designed.
The DMFCs are also beneficial in the application of portable devices as power generation resources. Moreover, the use of methanol instead of hydrogen as a fuel in fuel cells leads to several important benefits in the application of portable devices. Thus, methanol's liquid state volumetric energy density is superior to that of hydrogen's gas state.257,258 The energy range is very large running from low-watt to kilowatt power output.259 It is worth mentioning that there is much room in R&D for improvement in all branches: theory, fundamental physics and chemistry, experimental and technological aspects.260
Passive DMFCs are envisaged as appropriate sources of a wide range of portable devices with power consumption ranging from a few μW (watches) to tens of μW (pacemakers) to ∼100 W (computers like laptops) through a few W (smartphones).

4. Direct ethanol fuel cells (DEFCs)
4.1 Short overview
In order to overcome the problems with methanol (e.g., unsustainable fuel source, high toxicity, flammable and volatile substance), ethanol is a trustworthy fuel choice in non-H2 fuel cells including direct ethanol fuel cells (DEFCs), where it acts as an electron donor in the anode.261,262
DEFCs can be used in many applications such as electronic devices, home gadgets, and educational kits. This type of fuel cells also have potential to be used as power for vehicles, but financial support in this area is limited because hydrogen fuel cells have been the focus in this field.13,263
In the direct ethanol fuel cells, the principal goal is to convert ethanol to carbon dioxide in the oxidation reaction process and thereby, in a single reaction double the electrons can be produced compared with the DMFCs.264 The oxidation process varies in DEFCs depending on the catalyst type, fuel structure, membrane used and also parameters such as temperature and concentration.265 Therefore, more studies need to be completed towards an increase of the efficiency reported for DEFCs,.266
Ethanol possesses some important characteristics (e.g., it is a feasibly renewable fuel source that can be produced from a number of sources, especially biomass such as sugar cane, wheat, and corn,267,268 is a nontoxic alcohol,268 and has higher mass energy density than methanol (8.0 vs. 6.1 kW h kg−1)),269 which make it a suitable fuel for direct fuel cells. A very important viewpoint is that the carbon dioxide emitted from DEFCs can be consumed afterwards by plants, allowing in this way a zero greenhouse contribution to the atmosphere.268
To date, there are two major types of DEFC designs reported for the direct ethanol oxidation reaction (EOR): (a) proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEM-DEFCs) and (b) anion exchange membrane fuel cells (AEM-DEFCs).270 A schematic representation of these types of DEFCs is shown in Fig. 14.
 Fig. 14  Schematic design illustration of the DEFCs: (a) the proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEM-DEFC) and (b) the anion exchange membrane fuel cells (AEM-DEFC). At the anode side, there are two possible ways to perform the EOR. Note that the highest number of electrons is generated through the C1 pathway as compared to the C2 pathway. 
Aside from the PEM- and AEM-DEFCs, a new class has been developed consisting of an alkaline anode, a membrane (a cation-exchange membrane – CEM), and an acid cathode employing hydrogen peroxide as the oxidant, termed the alkaline–acid DEFC (AA-DEFC).270 With these new AA-DEFCs, the theoretical voltage increases270 up to 2.52 V (see Fig. 15) and it was experimentally proved that a peak power density of 360 mW cm−2 could be obtained at 60 °C,271 which represent the highest performances of DEFCs reported so far in the open literature. However, there are two critical issues related to the AA-DEFCs,272 such as species crossover (which creates a mixed potential and decreases the cell performance) and hydrogen peroxide decomposition (which causes a significant decrease in the cathode potential and thus dramatically decreases the cell performance), issues that might be mitigated by adopting a bifunctional electrode architecture.273,274
 Fig. 15  Theoretical voltage of direct ethanol-fuel cells, (a) alkaline fuel cell using O2 as the oxidant, (b) acid fuel cell using H2O2 as the oxidant, (c) alkaline–acid fuel cell using H2O2 as the oxidant. Reproduced with permission from ref. 270, Copyright 2011 Elsevier Publications. 
4.2 Acid type direct ethanol fuel cells (PEM-DEFCs)
Typically, a PEM-DEFC involves the use of a MEA, which is formed by sandwiching the PEM between an anode and a cathode. MEA, which is regarded as the “heart” of PEMFCs, contains 5 layers: (i) an anode diffusion layer (ADL) which, like most of the DLs, consists of two layers, a backing layer (BL) made of carbon paper, and a micro-porous layer (MPL), usually composed of a mixture of a hydrophobic polymer (usually PTFE) and carbon powder;275 these DLs allow efficient transfer of the reactants to the catalytic layer and proper evacuation of the produced compounds;276 (ii) an anode catalyst layer (ACL), which contains either a single catalyst or a mixture of catalysts and ionomers that provide triple phase boundaries (TPB) for the EOR; (iii) a PEM typically composed of either Nafion® 115 (ref. 277) or Nafion® 117 (ref. 278)-type produced by DuPont, which facilitates the transfer of the produced protons from the anode to the cathode, and physically acts as a separator between the anode and the cathode; (iv) a cathode catalyst layer (CCL) which contains ionomers and the catalyst for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR), and (v) a cathode diffusion layer (CDL). A schematic representation of a PEM-DEFC can be found in Fig. 14a.
The working principle is similar to that of a DMFC. Thus, the EOR takes place at the ACL with the generation of electrons, protons and different products of the electro-oxidation reaction. As stated, the EOR is believed to be a complex multi-step reaction that takes place through a dual-pathway mechanism, denoted as C1 and C2 in Fig. 12a, based on which the C1-pathway (higher electro-efficiency) corresponds to the total ethanol oxidation up to CO2 and the generation of 12 electrons and 12 protons (see eqn (2)), while the C2-pathway (lower electro-efficiency) represents the partial ethanol oxidation to acetic acid and/or acetaldehyde and the generation of 4 and/or 2 electrons/protons, respectively (see eqn (3) and (4)): C2H5OH + 3H2O → 2CO2 + 12H+ + 12e−(2) C2H5OH + H2O → CH3COOH + 4H+ + 4e−(3) C2H5OH → CH3CHO + 2H+ + 2e−(4)
It should be noted that, with the state-of-the-art catalysts, the products of the EOR are a mixture of acetic acid, acetaldehyde and CO2.279–281 Thus, the EOR taking place on the anode will theoretically follow eqn (5): C2H5OH + 4/3H2O → 1/3CH3COOH + 1/3CH3CHO + 2/3CO2 + 6H+ + 6e−(5)
However, in practice, the amount of acetic acid and acetaldehyde formed is always higher than that of CO2.282 Moreover, it is commonly accepted that the total oxidation of ethanol might not be possible at temperatures below 90 °C.283 Therefore, in a more realistic way and taking into account that the ORR takes place at the cathode, the overall reaction in PEM-DEFCs will follow eqn (6) rather than the well-known eqn (7): C2H5OH + 3/4O2 → 1/2CH3COOH + 1/2CH3CHO + H2O(6) C2H5OH + 3O2 → 2CO2 + 3H2O(7)
As already specified, the EOR is a complex process which, in order to provide better electro-efficiency and performance, requires the cleavage of one C–C bond (through the C1-pathway). To realize this process in acid media, however, an extremely active catalyst should be used and, although tremendous efforts have been expended in developing such materials (mainly Pt-based catalysts at both the anode and cathode), the performance of PEM-DEFCs remains low.284 The low performance of PEM-DEFCs (i.e., the state-of-the-art PPDs obtained at 90 °C are 79.5 mW cm−2, 82 mW cm−2 and 96 mW cm−2 on PtRu/C, PtSn/C–B and Pt3Sn/C–PtRu/C double-layered anode catalysts, respectively),285–287 as listed in Table 9, is a consequence of the sluggish kinetics of the EOR which leads to huge activation loss. On the other hand, another process that influences the performance of the PEM-DEFCs is the crossover effect, which is related to the movement of ethanol from the anode to the cathode through the PEM. Due to this process, there will be two parallel and competitive reactions taking place on the cathode (ORR and the EOR) and a mixed potential will be generated.288

When talking about the performances, it was proved that, at low-temperature, PtSn/C yielded superior performance in comparison to the more common Pt/C or even hybrid PtRu/C catalysts.289 Even so, the electro-oxidation of ethanol on PtSn/C leads to the formation of partially oxidized products (acetaldehyde and acetic acid), while the total oxidation to CO2 was not improved. Still, the improvement of the current density achieved might be attributed to the presence of the PtSn alloy that changes the geometric and electronic structure of Pt (an extension of the lattice parameter) which accelerates the adsorption of ethanol (rate-determining step of the EOR) on the surface of the catalyst together with the easier removal of the CO-like species from the surface.285
In the view of these aspects, many other studies comprising the use of PtSn/C,287,288,290–294 Pt2Sn/C295,296 or Pt3Sn/C283,297 catalysts were conducted in the last two decades. Much more than that, a simple model, which can predict the performance of a single DEFC by varying the Sn content and the degree of alloying platinum and tin, was developed.298,299 Other binary Pt-based electrocatalysts, such as PtRu,75,300–305 PtPd306,307 and PtRh308–310 among others, were used as anodes in the electro-oxidation of ethanol in acidic media.
Ternary Pt-based electrocatalysts, such as Pt3Sn1P2 (ref. 311) and PtRhSn/C,312,313 gave interesting results. The deposition of Pt and Rh on carbon-supported SnO2 nanoparticles was used for the preparation of ternary PtRhSnO2/C electrocatalysts with varied Pt:Rh:Sn ratios capable of catalyzing the total oxidation of ethanol in acidic media.312,313 Thus, as the authors stated, the Pt–Rh–SnO2/C electrocatalyst with the atomic ratio of Pt:Rh:Sn = 3:1:4 showed the highest activity; this material was capable of breaking the C–C bond within the ethanol molecule even at room temperature (as was verified by the in situ infrared reflection-absorption spectroscopy experiments employed in this study). In the case of the Rh-free catalyst (Pt–SnO2/C catalyst, with Pt:Sn = 3:4 atomic ratio), acetic acid was obtain as the major product. The authors concluded that, for the cleavage of the C–C bond in ethanol, the use of all three constituents is necessary, since the synergistic effect between them facilitates the total oxidation of ethanol.312
To summarize till now, when discussing about the anodic catalysts used in the PEM-DEFCs, it should be noted that Pt itself is rapidly poisoned by strongly adsorbed species coming from the dissociative adsorption of organic molecules, with the removal of these species requiring potentials higher than 0.5–0.6 V.314 Modification of the electrode surface in such a way as to increase its coverage in oxygenated species, like adsorbed OH, is beneficial for the removal of the CO-like species from the surface. However, this process should take place at low potentials and thus, accomplishing it requires the alloying of Pt with other metals that are sufficiently stable in acidic media. In this aspect, the large number of multifunctional electrocatalysts presented above is a step forward in the development of the PEM-DEFC field toward practical use.
4.3 Alkaline type DEFCs (AEM-DEFCs)
In an alkaline DEFC, anions are formed at the cathode reaction and flow towards the anode through an AEM (typically A201 by Tokuyama) (see Fig. 14a). The primary duty of the AEM is to functionally conduct hydroxyl ions and, furthermore, act as a separator between them. Considering the absence of specifically absorbed ions in alkaline solutions and the higher coverage of adsorbed OH at low potentials,315 the activity in alkaline media is enhanced. This is a major advantage that allows the use of low catalyst loadings and also permits selection from a wide range of catalysts including nonprecious metals.
One more significant advantage is the reduced alcohol crossover, because the conducting pathway of OH anions from the cathode to the anode through the membrane is opposite to the direction of the electroosmotic drag. Fuel cell performance can be considerably improved as a result of faster kinetics of both ethanol oxidation reaction (EOR) and oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in alkaline media, even when inexpensive metal catalysts are used.275 In addition, the charge carriers from alkaline media (i.e. OH−) pull up both molecules back to the anode, alleviating the ethanol and water crossovers.317 The most interesting characteristics of the AEM-based concept are the less corrosive environment, longer durability than the PEM-DEFC model and, last but not least, the faster kinetics of the ORR, permitting the use of noble metal-free catalysts.318Fig. 16 shows the illustration of such a fuel cell.
 Fig. 16  Graphic representation of an AEM-DFC with the: (A) theoretical and (B) real electrode reactions taking place at the electrodes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 316, Copyright 2019 Elsevier Publications. 
Thus, the fuel solution is carried through the anode diffusion layer to the anode catalyst layer, where ethanol is oxidized (EOR) with a theoretical cell voltage of −0.74 V to hypothetically produce electrons, water and carbon dioxide319 and the associated electrochemical reactions occurring at the respective electrode interfaces considering complete oxidation of the ethanol as presented in Fig. 16A. The anode oxidation reaction is counterbalanced at the cathode by the ORR, generating a theoretical cell voltage of 1.14 V. However, in practice, ethanol is known to be partially oxidized and the process may proceed exclusively to acetaldehyde or acetic acid (acetate in alkaline media), giving only 2 or 4 electrons.275,320 Thus, the electron transfer number is reduced to 4 per ethanol molecule, making the electron transfer rate lower316 (see Fig. 16B).
The EOR mechanism in alkaline media is presented in Fig. 17. Thus, the partial ethanol oxidation route follows the C2 pathway321 (see Fig. 17A), while the more challenging C–C bond breaking process is achieved by following the C1 reaction pathway (see Fig. 17B).
 Fig. 17  Alkaline-based ethanol oxidation reaction mechanism: (A) C2 and (B) C1 pathways. Reproduced with permission from ref. 316, Copyright 2019 Elsevier Publications. 
In ethanol electro-oxidation, breaking the C–C bond requires the development of efficient catalysts in order to obtain the optimum composition, morphology, size and shape specifically for the desired reaction.323 The catalytic performances of the electrocatalysts can be influenced by many factors,324 as represented in Fig. 18.
 Fig. 18  Different factors and parameters that can control the catalyst performances. Reproduced with permission from ref. 322, Copyright 2013 Elsevier Publications. 

4.3.1 Pt based EOR electrocatalysts for AEM-DEFCs. 
Even though many different types of anode catalysts are used, current research is focused on overcoming the difficulty of cleaving the C–C bond from ethanol molecule.325 The presence of such a strong bond leads to difficulties in complete achievement of the reaction of ethanol and thus, lowers the reaction efficiency. Many anode electrocatalysts have been studied for AEM DEFCs, but the most frequently used ones are those based on platinum,326 and some representative examples are listed in Table 10.

As shown, the anode catalysts mainly contain Pt327–329 or Pt alloyed with other metals like Ru, Pd or Cu. The alloying process decreases the costs, while the activity and stability of the catalysts are improved. These benefits have been attributed to the synergistic effects between the metal constituents and to the bifunctional mechanism that ease the desorption of carbonaceous species that poison the catalyst. Thus, it was proved that the lattice parameters and the electronic properties of Pt at the surface can be modified by the second metal, and the resulting catalyst possesses higher activity and longer stability.330
Based on the fact that alkaline DAFCs suffer from a rapid decrease in cell potential at low discharge currents, Ünlü et al.331 performed an interesting study in which the performance of the anionic electrodes in polymer-based alkaline fuel cells is analysed. Thus, the performance of an alkaline DEFC with a PtRu/C anode (loading 4 mgPtRu cm−2), a Tokuyama A201 AEM and a 30 wt% Pt/C cathode (loading 1 mgPt cm−2) was characterized with and without the presence of excess KOH in the anode fuel. The authors showed that in the absence of KOH (only 1 M ethanol) the maximum power density was 1.7 mW cm−2, while, when KOH (0.25 M KOH) was added to the ethanol solution at the anode, the PPD increased to 22.4 mW cm−2. At the same time, with increase of the pH at the anode, the OCP improved from 0.68 V to 0.85 V.
The authors noticed that at very low current densities the cell potential sharply drops, while when the current reached the mass transfer limited regime a modest drop in cell potential occurs.331 Such an abrupt drop in potential was also observed in other studies. For instance, Fujiwara et al.332 reported a PPD of 58 mW cm−2 at 25 °C by using 1 M EtOH and 0.5 M KOH as the fuel in a DEFC with a PtRu/C anode (loading 3 mgPtRu cm−2), a Tokuyama AEM and Pt/C cathode (loading 3 mgPt cm−2), while in the absence of KOH the same cell yielded only 8 mW cm−2.
With the aim of evaluating the alkaline stability, different alternatives to the conventional AEMs were studied and reported in several papers. Thus, recently, Zakaria et al.333 have produced thin films of modified quaternized polyvinyl alcohol (QPVA) with graphene oxide and used them in alkaline DEFCs comprising Pt–Ru/C anode catalysts (loading 2 mg cm−2), and Pt/C cathode catalysts (loading 2 mg cm−2). The QPVA/GO membrane soaked in 4 M KOH for 24 h at 80 °C achieved high proton conductivity (0.021 S cm−1) and low ethanol permeability 1.245 × 10−7 cm2 s−1.
The authors have thus demonstrated the stability (ca. 1000 h) of these AEMs under extreme alkaline conditions and through hydrolysis, the achieved PPD at ambient temperature being 16.88 mW cm−2. These results were a significant improvement of the performances obtained by Shaari et al.340 wherein they achieved a PPD of 11.28 at 70 °C by using a similar alkaline DEFC system.
In the same context, Osmieri et al.343 performed a study in which four carbonaceous materials (acetylene black, multi-walled carbon nanotubes, and two carbon materials synthesized by the hard-template method) were used as supports to synthesize four Fe–N/C cathode catalysts. Although the performances of these Fe–N/C catalysts were very promising (72.8 mW cm−2), the stability of the alkaline DEFC was low. The instability issues were tentatively attributed by the authors to several identified factors: (i) the instability of the MEAs used in the cell; (ii) the incompatibility between the ionomer used in the preparation ink and the membrane, (iii) the rapid formation of water as the reaction product at the anode, and (iv) the detachment/separation of the electrodes from the membrane which can be caused by the absence of hot pressing during the MEA preparation.
Interesting results were obtained under alkaline conditions when a commercial polybenzimidazole membrane doped with OH− (PBI/OH−) was used as the electrolyte,335,338,341,342,344 all of these studies demonstrating the use of PtRu/C anode catalysts.
To summarize, the production of highly electroactive and durable catalysts for the EOR and the ORR are still challenging and future studies should focus on finding solutions to the alkaline DEFC issues.


4.3.2 Pt-free based EOR electrocatalysts for AEM-DEFCs. 
Although Pt is the most common electrocatalyst used as the anode, due to its ability to oxidize a wide range of fuels, its limitation is related to the high cost and availability. Moreover, Pt is prone to poisoning with the intermediate formed during ethanol oxidation. Therefore, other alternatives are being searched and a viable solution is the use of Pd that has high ethanol oxidation activity under alkaline conditions, high poisoning tolerance and high stability at low temperatures.319 Moreover, Tran et al.348 demonstrated that the conventional Pd/C as the anode, Pt/C as the cathode and A201 as the AEM are very good candidates to assemble a cell with very good performance at 60 °C (128 mW cm−2 for ethanol) capable of supporting different liquid fuels (1-propanol, 2-propanol, ethylene glycol, propylene glycol or glycerol) and achieving good activity. The fuel stream contains ethanol and KOH, otherwise in the absence of the base, the power density decreases to 9 mW cm−2. However, the use of monometallic Pd for DEFCs as the anode is an issue, despite its great aforementioned abilities, because Pd particle size grows over time and degrades the DEFC. Therefore, intense research was made to highlight the good features of Pd in alkaline DEFCs and the use of Pd-based composites (alloyed or in other metallic combinations) has gained ground, mainly due to the bifunctional mechanism observed when metals or metal oxides were added on a carbon black support, among others.349,350
Pt-free anodes have been reviewed previously,275 therefore in this section we will point out only the most performant DEFCs and the advantages brought by the development of new materials for electrodes, new membranes or new ways to assemble MEAs. In comparison with the results presented for Pt-based DEFC anodes (see Table 10), when Pt-free anodes are used, an improvement of the fuel cell performance is observed (see Table 11).
