As shown in Table 10, alloying Pt with Ru brings several advantages, for instance, high activity due to the low particle size, which influences the DEFC performance.351,352 Ma et al.352 confirmed that alloying Pd and Ru is also a great choice. They have designed a fuel cell using a MnO2 nanotube catalyst prepared in-house as the cathode and A201 as the AEM. In comparison with a Pd/C catalyst with the same particle size, Pd3Ru/C was 1.8 times superior in the practical fuel cell with a PPD of 176 mW cm−2 at 80 °C.
A Pt anode alloyed with Au for DEFCs shows increased stability, but did not present any improvement in ethanol electroactivity. However, Xu et al.353 demonstrated that Pd behaves differently than Pt when alloyed with Au and improves the electro-oxidation of ethanol, thus resulting in more stable DEFCs. This behaviour can be due to the special ability of Au to withdraw electrons from the neighbouring Pd atoms. In the DEFC, the stability increased with the increase of Au content, but was in detriment of the activity, therefore a compromise between the two features should be done. The authors concluded that the composition Pd3Au/C maintains a good cell performance (53.8 mW cm−2 at 40 °C) equivalent to that of the Pd/C anode, and at the same time improves the stability of the DEFC (a loss of 25% in current density after 3000 cycles compared with 53% loss when Pd/C was used, under the same conditions).
Considering that the MEA assembly of AEM DEFCs was derived from acid DAFCs, Li et al.354 tried to increase the performance of DEFCs by improving the anode electrode structure. Thus, the DEFC fabricated using a non-platinum Fe–Co HYPERMEC™ K14 (Acta) cathode, a A-201 membrane provided by Tokuyama, and an in-house prepared anode (PdNi/C) achieved a power density of 130 mW cm−2 at 80 °C at a current density of 1060 mA cm−2. The difference between the conventional anode and the integrated anode structure proposed consists in adopting a modified way of putting the catalysts in contact with the Ni foam.
The Ni foam skeleton was dip-coated in a solution containing the catalyst and the ink, while the conventional method involves brushing of the Ni foam with the prepared catalyst ink. Changing the method leads to a good dispersion of the catalyst onto the Ni foam, an enhanced transport of reactants/products and ion charges and a reduced cell resistance. The same group tried a layered reduction method when Pd was deposited on the Ni foam, and succeeded in increasing the DEFC performance to 164 mW cm−2.375
In 2011, An et al.379 demonstrated that very good performances can also be obtained if, instead of the anion exchange membrane, a cation exchange membrane to conduct Na+ (found in the fuel stream) from the anode to the cathode is used. The DEFC assembly consists of a PdNi/C anode, Fe–Co K-14 HYPERMEC™ (ACTA) cathode and AEM or CEM as the exchange membrane. The performance of the CEM is slightly higher (135 mW cm−2) than that of AEM (115 mW cm−2) at 90 °C, and seems to be more stable with temperature (AEM starts to degrade at temperatures >60 °C). However, using a cation exchange membrane also brings other disadvantages that need to be addressed, like NaOH formation at the cathode.
Not only alloying Pd, but also addition of a metal oxide could boost the DEFC performance. It has been assumed that the oxophilicity of the metal oxides has the ability to reduce the Pd loading and to provide the hydroxyl groups required for the oxidation of ethanol molecules adsorbed onto the surface.360 Supporting the Pd nanoparticles on CeO2, yields twice as much higher performance of the DEFC (140 mW cm−2), compared with the conventional Pd/C and confirms that Pd–OHads species formed on the ceria support are responsible for the enhanced DEFC performance.365 Trimetallic anodes (PdNiZn) were also investigated in passive DEFCs having a polymer electrolyte membrane and Fe–Co K-14 Hypermec™ as the cathode. The PEM-DEFC performance was high (170 mW cm−2) and was associated with the synergy between Ni and Zn, which promoted the increase of OHads species on the catalyst surface.374
Very good performance of the DEFC was obtained by replacing the conventional Hypermec™ or Pt/C cathode with a modified Pd–Au alloy supported on carbon nanotubes as the cathode (185 mW cm−2).376 Also, on modifying the carbon support with nitrogen, when Pd was used as the anode, excellent performance of bthe DEFC at 60 °C was obtained (a PPD of 202 mW cm−2).370 This increase in power density is explained by a higher dispersion of Pd nanoparticles when the support is decorated with nitrogen. Similar results (202.3 mW cm−2) were obtained by using a bimetallic PdAu anode supported on carbon nanotubes with the note that higher temperature (i.e., 80 °C) was used.359


4.3.3 Graphene based support for electrocatalysts used for DEFCs. 
To further increase the applicability of the materials used as anodes or cathodes described in the previous sections, the nanoparticles of noble metals are dispersed on high surface area supports, like carbon black or carbon nanotubes. Recently, graphenes and their derivatives belonging to this class of materials were found as promising alternatives to the classical carbon-based supports. Therefore, as a strategy to minimize the metal-loading and to improve the electro-oxidation of ethanol toward commercialization of DEFCs, graphene-like materials have been increasingly considered as supports. We will further discuss the design improvements in graphene-based electrodes for ethanol electro-oxidation.
Graphenes. 
Graphenes are 2D materials with single or few atomic layers, high surface area, good electrical conductivity and chemical activity.380–382 With the progress in the field of graphene materials and the developed properties, they have been applied in fuel cell as supports for anodes and the major improvement is better metal dispersion in contact with the support, which induces much higher electroactivity compared with the analogues supported on carbon black.34,383,384 Several studies underlined the advantages of metals supported on graphene, such as Pt380,381,385 or Pd,382,386 however many others emphasized that alloying the metal will bring more important gains when used for the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction. Thus, PtPd,387 PdAg,388,389 PdNi,360 NiCo,390 and even trimetallic combinations like PtRhFe391 pointed out to an improvement of the electrocatalysts for the ethanol electro-oxidation. Involvement of two metals has proven to result in several favourable outcomes such as good catalytic activity, better stability and also different selectivity. All these features are related to different phenomena when the two metals are associated, such as modified geometry of the active site, changed electronic state, improved stability and the presence of a synergetic or bi-functional effect.392
Nevertheless, the use of only graphene as the support for the metal nanoparticles has proven to not completely address the issues concerning the high dispersibility of the metallic nanoparticles during the deposition process. Thus, the next step was to modify graphene in order to improve the anodes in alkaline DEFCs.
Modified graphenes. 
Graphene can be modified in various ways in order to improve the performance of the direct ethanol fuel cell. For this purpose different molecules can be used to modify the graphene surface and can be classified into non-covalent and covalent functions of the bond type formed with the surface.393
The presence of functional groups on graphene nanosheets was found to increase the wettability and the accessibility of the metal nanoparticles to the electroactive surface, by acting as nucleation centers and anchoring sites and thus limiting the particle growth.394–396
Moreover, using graphene oxides as supports, due to the presence of OH groups on the surface, a composite with the functionalized group and metallic nanoparticles is formed. Such a composite, owing to its triple junction structure, has an increased surface area, and better metal dispersion and electrical conductivity.382,397
Doped graphenes. 
Doping graphene with other hetero-atoms was among the first attempts to improve its properties. It was found that insertion of nitrogen in graphene produces structural changes and electronic rearrangement of the graphene sheets, which induces the appearance of new active sites in the graphene structure.398–400 Additionally, several other benefits were confirmed during different studies: N-doping can reduce the particle size, improve the anchoring of the catalysts onto the electrode, and assure a greater dispersion of the nanoparticles, all these resulting in an electrocatalyst with higher performance. For example, Wu et al.399 demonstrated that N-doped graphene generates a synergetic effect with the supported metal, which results in four times increased electro-oxidation activity for PdPb/NG (152.3 mA cm−2) compared with Pd/G (38.0 mA cm−2). The main reason for this behaviour is the very good dispersibility of the metals when the support contains a large amount of nitrogen as can be seen in Fig. 19. In another attempt, insertion of two heterogeneous atoms, B and N, into graphene was tried and it succeeded in improving the electrochemical activity and stability of the electrodes in the ethanol electro-oxidation. Also, Chowdhury et al.401 reported a Pd/BN-GNR modified electrocatalyst prepared in one-step with improved performance compared to Pd/GNRs and Pd/C in ethanol electro-oxidation (see Fig. 19). All these reports claim that the synergy between the co-doped/doped-graphene and the metallic nanoparticles is responsible for the enhancement of the electroactivity due to the good dispersibility of the metal onto the surface, increase of catalytic active sites and increase of conductivity.
 Fig. 19  (a) TEM images of Pd/G (A1/A2) and Pd/NG (B1/B2) and the particle size distribution of Pd/G (A3) and Pd/NG (B3); (b) CO stripping curves on Pd/G, Pd/NG and PdPb/NG (8:1.0) in 1 M KOH solution. Scan rate: 50 mV s−1; (c) cyclic voltammograms of Pd/C, PdPb/C (8:1.0), Pd/G, Pd/NG, PdPb/G (8:1.0) and PdPb/NG (8:1.0) in the 1 M KOH + 1 M C2H5OH solution. Scan rate: 50 mV s−1;399 (d) schematic illustration of fabrication for Pd/BN-GNRs; TEM images of (e) Pd/GNRs and (f) Pd/BN-GNRs, inset shows the size distribution of the Pd NPs; (g) CV of Pd-based catalysts in 1.0 M NaOH + 1.0 M C2H5OH solution at a scan rate of 50 mV s−1, (h) chronoamperometric curves for the Pd-based catalysts in 1.0 M NaOH + 1.0 M C2H5OH solution at −0.20 V.402 Reproduced with permissions from ref. 399 and 402, respectively, Copyrights 2015 and 2016, respectively, Elsevier Publications and The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Nanocomposites: metal oxides–graphenes. 
Previous studies have shown that the combination of noble metals with metal oxides yields more active and stable electrocatalysts for the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction.403–407 The main disadvantage of these electrocatalysts is related to the poor conductivity of metal oxides, which hinders their use in the application as supports. One way to surpass this issue is to design electrocatalysts that take the advantage of the good electronic conductivity of graphenes. Some examples of such triple composition noble metals/metallic oxides/graphene are reviewed below.
The first attempt to improve the properties of graphene by adding metal oxides to form a nanocomposite was made by Wen et al.382 They succeeded in preparing a new composite by combining the properties of SnO2, graphene oxide and a Pd precursor (Pd/SnO2-GNS). The Pd/SnO2-GNS presents higher catalytic activity compared with the Pd/GNS catalyst in ethanol oxidation, and this enhanced activity was due to the high ECSA. The good dispersibility of Pd particles when the SnO2-GNS support vs. GNS was used has been emphasized by TEM analysis (see Fig. 20).
 Fig. 20  TEM images of (a) GO, (b) SnO2-GNS, (c) Pd/GNS and (d) Pd/SnO2-GNS. The inset image of (b) is the high-resolution TEM image of SnO2-GNS. Reproduced with permission from ref. 382, Copyright 2013 Elsevier Publications. 
Few years later, Qu et al.408 found a way to improve the electro-oxidation activity of noble metals deposited on SnO2/graphene by designing a layer-by-layer SnO2/Pt/graphene anode using a mild microwave assisted polyol process (MAPP). They have also shown that the electro-oxidation process of ethanol depends on the amount of graphene deposited. 30 wt% of graphene was found to be the optimum amount for the developed compositions.
Cerium oxide was also considered to improve the activity of Me/graphene electrocatalysts.409 Thus, the strategy chosen to improve the electroactivity was to alloy Pt with cerium oxide in order to reduce the amount of Pt, together with the modification of the reduced graphene surface by anchoring –SO3H groups. The role of sulfonic groups on the graphene surface was to improve the Pt dispersion by increasing the hydrophilicity of the support. The results showed that the employed approach was successful with an increase of 17% of current density comparing Pt/CeO2/SRGO with Pt/SRGO, which is in agreement with the proposed Tempkin mechanism.
The use of manganese oxide and a bimetallic alloy (Pd–Ni–MnO2/rGO)410 or of a magnetic spinel in a composition of the type Pd/CoFe2O4/rGO presents a corroborative effect in the presence of the metal and graphene support and yields electrocatalysts with higher stability and CO tolerance and also with enhanced electroactivity.
Shamraiz et al.411 decided to modify the Pd d-band centers with earth-abundant alkaline metals. According to the d-band theory, the Pd adsorption capacity is enhanced with the uplift of d-band centers and therefore using earth-alkaline metals, like Ca, which possesses a higher lattice constant (5.58 Å) than Pd (3.89 Å) and has the potential to uplift the Pd d-band centers. Such changes in d-band will produce improvement in the oxidation of alcohols at more negative potentials. Undoubtedly, the presence of oxygen from CaO during the doping process helped in overcoming the adsorbed intermediate poisoning issues occurring on carbonaceous materials. The enhanced PdCa/rGO mass activity (4674.70 mA mg−1 Pd) compared with Pd/rGO (1490.08 mA mg−1 Pd) was also related to the homogeneous distribution of Ca and Pd on graphene sheets which resulted in high activity, stability and increased tolerance to CO poisoning of the electrocatalyst.
Functionalized graphenes. 
Noncovalent anchors, for noble metal modified graphenes, can boost the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction. In the following section examples such as poly(diallyldimethylammonium chloride) (PDDA), perylene tetracarboxylic acid (PTCA), poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) (PEDOT), dimethyldiallylammonium chloride (DMDAAC), polyaniline (PANI), and polypyrrole (PPy) are reviewed. In Fig. 21 some examples of the synthesis method of polymer/graphene based electrocatalysts are schematically represented.
 Fig. 21  Schematic representation of the synthesis of (a) Pd–PDDA/rGO,412 (b) Pd/DMDAAC–rGO,397 (c) PtSn/PEDOT/EGO,415 and (d) Pt–Pd/PPy/NGO.420 Reproduced with permissions from ref. 412, 397, 415, and 420, Copyrights 2015, 2016, 2018 and 2018, respectively, Elsevier Publications. 
PDDA was used to design and prepare a monometallic composite like Pd–PDDA/rGO412 or a bimetallic composite electrode such as PDDA–rGO–PdPt via the sonoelectrochemical method.413 The use of PDDA in both cases increased the ethanol electro-oxidation and the stability of the electrocatalyst mainly due to the good dispersion of PDDA and the metal onto the graphene surface. The current density of Pd–PDDA/rGO developed for ethanol oxidation is 1.38 times higher than that of the Pd/rGO homologue,412 while for PDDA–rGO–PdPt the current density (6.7 mA cm−2) is nearly 1.5 times higher than that of the PdPt alloy without rGO.413 The high homogeneity of PDDA has also been found to increase the graphene conductivity and to improve the CO tolerance. Moreover, the sonoelectrochemical method used by Shi et al.413 plays an important role in graphene sheet separation, thus combining the special feature of 2D graphene with the metallic alloy properties.
Li et al.414 studied the influence of PTCA when coating the graphene sheets. The authors demonstrated the successful functionalization of graphene with PTCA taking advantage of π–π stacking and hydrophobic forces. The Pd/PTCA–GS (NaBH4) electrocatalyst prepared presented enhanced electroactivity for ethanol electro-oxidation, which can be mainly correlated with the narrower size distribution, better homogeneity and uniform crystal structure. Several studies415–418 reported an increased electroactivity when PEDOT was used as the polymer to obtain graphene nanocomposites. Its success is mainly due to its high conductivity and very good stability in the oxidized state in comparison with other conductive polymers. Pt/PEDOT/ER-GO composites present a maximum mass peak current density of 78.7% which was better than that of the Pt/C electrode.418 The advantage of this composite is the long-term stability in acidic medium and very good tolerance to CO poisoning but also the fact that it is prepared by a greener route416 in order to increase its applicability.415 Studies on PEDOT/rGO were extended to the use of Pd as the noble metal417 and the big plus was the one-pot facile synthesis of the nanocomposites, since their synthesis can be complex and laborious. Such materials are found to be very stable and have enhanced electrocatalytic activity compared with Pd–PEDOT and Pd/C catalysts.
Zhang et al.397 demonstrated that the use of DMDAAC, a cationic polymer, has several advantages: it stabilizes the metallic nanoparticles, avoids graphene nanosheet agglomeration and increases the conductivity. It has been found that Pd/DMDAAC-rGO has ∼7 times higher electrocatalytic activity and stability (3.25 × 10−2 mA cm−2 exchanged current density) than Pd/rGO for ethanol oxidation (4.87 × 10−3 mA cm−2).397 One of the main reasons for this behaviour is that DMDAAC assists the formation of small Pd nanoparticles and better dispersion at high metal concentrations, when it is supported on rGO.
Recently, polyaniline and polypyrrole were employed to enhance the performance of electrocatalysts for ethanol electro-oxidation. Besides the good conductivity and stability, polyaniline is also known have good intrinsic flexibility.419 The insertion of PANI into the Au/PANI/rGO electrode, by a facile and rapid strategy, will increase the peak current density by 3.5 times when compared with the Au/rGO electrode, probably due to a better charge transport phenomenon. A similar effect was found when PPy was used as an anchor for the deposition of the catalysts onto the electrode.
Ni nanoparticles421 or bimetallic Pt–Pd noble metals420 were used as noble metals in combination with PPy and graphene oxide to produce electrocatalysts for ethanol electro-oxidation. Both material combinations, besides the favourable effect over ethanol electro-oxidation (21.1 mA cm−2 current density for NiNPs-R/PPy/CPE),421 present good tolerance to CO and other intermediates resulting from the ethanol oxidation.

4.4 Alkaline–acid direct ethanol fuel cells (AA-DEFCs)
As described above, the conventional DEFCs operating in both acid and alkaline media, show several limitations, such as: (i) the sluggish kinetics of the EOR in the case of PEM-DEFCs; (ii) about one order of magnitude lower ionic conductivity of the AEM compared to that of Nafion® membranes; (iii) poor thermal stability of the AEM, and (iv) the low theoretical voltage (1.14 V). The increase of theoretical voltage can be achieved by changing the conventional oxidant (O2/air) to a better one (H2O2). Thus, as shown in Fig. 15, while the anode potentials of both the PEM- and AEM-DEFC operating with H2O2 (as the oxidant) remain the same (i.e., 0.09 V for PEM-DEFC and −0.74 V for AEM-DEFC), the cathode potentials of both the PEM- and AEM-DEFC increase from 1.23 V to 1.78 V and from 0.40 V to 0.87 V, respectively.270 Based on these aspects, combining the lower anode potential of the AEM-DEFC and the higher cathode potential of PEM-DEFC, at the same time with the use of hydrogen peroxide as the oxidant, will result in the formation of a new cell characterized by a higher theoretical voltage (2.52 V) and faster kinetics of the ethanol oxidation reaction. The working principle of this fuel cell will be detailed in the next section.
The use of hydrogen peroxide, however, implies the appearance of three major issues that reduce the performance. The first one refers to the drastic drop in the cathode potential due to the H2O2 decomposition. This decomposition process led to the formation of H2O and O2, which further creates the premise for simultaneous appearance of both hydrogen peroxide reduction reaction (HPRR) and oxygen reduction reaction at the cathode. As the potential associated with each reaction is different (lower in the case of the ORR than that of the HPRR), a mixed potential is created. The second issue is caused by the release of gaseous O2 (through H2O2 decomposition) which led to a two-phase mass transport in the cathode (the mass transport resistance of liquid-phase H2O2 in the cathode electrode is much larger than that of gaseous O2) and an increase in the gas void fraction, which lower the effective active area.422 In the same time, the H2O2 decomposition will cause a large concentration loss in the cathode catalyst layer. The third issue refers to the use of metal catalysts, such as platinum, palladium or gold which are required in order to minimize/lower the rate of the homogeneous decomposition of H2O2.423 It must be noted that decomposition of H2O2 can proceed homogeneously and heterogeneously. Homogeneous decomposition is caused by the intrinsic instability of H2O2 and the rate depends on several parameters, such as temperature, concentration and the solution pH value, among other minor factors. The rate of heterogeneous decomposition, however, depends on the specific catalyst that is used at the cathode. Thus, it was experimentally shown that the best metal catalysts, which reduce the rate of homogeneous decomposition, are mainly the noble metal-based ones.20 The use of such type of metals will maintain the total cost of the AA-DEFCs high and, thus, is yet seen as an issue and future studies are required to overcome it.
In 2011, An et al.270 reported for the first time the development of a new type of DEFC, named alkaline–acid DEFC (AA-DEFC), which was composed of an alkaline anode (PdNi/C with a loading of 1.0 mg cm−2), a membrane (a sodium conducting membrane based on pre-treated Nafion® 117) and an acid cathode (60 wt% Pt/C from Johnson-Matthey with a loading of 3.9 mg cm−2). The anolyte was an aqueous solution of ethanol (3 M) and sodium hydroxide (5 M), while the catholyte was an aqueous solution of hydrogen peroxide (4 M) and sulfuric acid (1 M), both flowing at 2 mL min−1 through the flow channels present at the anode and cathode, respectively. The fuel cell was able to achieve a PPD of 240 mW cm−2 at 60 °C with an OCV of 1.6 V. The large difference between this OCV and theoretical voltage (2.52 V) was mainly caused by the mixed potential phenomenon occurring on the cathode.
In the same year, An et al.271 have investigated the effects of MEA thickness and the concentrations of various species on the performance of the AA-DEFC working with the same anode and cathode like in the previous study. The authors found that the hydrogen peroxide concentration, ethanol concentration, NaOH concentration, and membrane thickness, have significant influences on the cell performance, reaching, with the MEA (a sodium conducting membrane based on pre-treated Nafion® 211) possessing a thickness of 25 μm, a PPD of 360 mW cm−2.
Once again, in 2011, An et al.,273 only by exchanging the cathode (a bifunctional cathode: Au/Ni–Cr foam with a loading of 1.2 mg cm−2) of the cell improved the direct electro-reduction of H2O2 and lowered the H2O2 decomposition rate and the mass transport resistance; in this way the AA-DEFC yielded a PPD of 200 mW cm−2 at 60 °C.
In 2014, An et al.424 proposed an approach to avoid the mixed-potential phenomenon by introducing a redox couple of V(IV)/V(V) to the cathode while using hydrogen peroxide to chemically charge the redox ions, as illustrated in Fig. 22. Faster cathodic electrochemical kinetics was achieved even with no noble metal catalysts. This new alkaline–acid DEFC yields a peak power density of 450 mW cm−2 at 60 °C.
 Fig. 22  Schematic representation of the ethanol-hydrogen peroxide fuel cells. Reproduced from ref. 424, Copyright 2014 Royal Society of Chemistry. 
A comparison of the above specified AA-DEFC performances is presented in Table 12.

5. Direct glycerol fuel cells (DGFCs)
5.1 Short overview
Glycerol is a surplus by-product obtained during the biodiesel manufacturing process by the transesterification of plant oils and animal fats and it accounts for 10% of the total product.425 Considered an ideal bio-renewable feedstock, the global glycerol market size was valued at 2.6 billion $ in 2019 and is expected to grow at a CAGR of 4.0% from 2020 to 2027.426 Moreover, the Oleoline Glycerine Market Report estimated that the price of crude glycerine will fall from $0.12 in June 2020 to $0.10 per pound by the end of 2021.427
Therefore, intensive research has been invested to develop processes based on glycerol, such as different catalytic methods to produce added value chemicals,428,429 but also processes to produce energy, for instance in fuel cell applications.321 In fact, glycerol was proposed for the first time in 1964 as a fuel for fuel cells430 due to its higher energy density (5965 W h L−1) comparable with that of methanol (5897 W h L−1) and close to that of ethanol (6307 W h L−1), and demonstrated certain advantages such as low-fuel cross-over and high-power density.431,432 Since then, fuel cells directly fed with glycerol, named direct glycerol fuel cells (DGFCs) have recently gained significant interest and are a current focus in the fuel cell research community.433,434
DGFCs are classified into the following major types, schematically represented in Fig. 23: (i) alkaline type, using an anion exchange membrane (AEM-DGFCs) in which the OH− anion is transported from the cathode to the anode; (ii) acid type, using a proton exchange membrane (PEM-DGFCs), in which the H+ is transported from the anode to the cathode, and (iii) alkaline–acid type, which uses a cation exchange membrane (AA-DGFCs) and brings together the advantages of both alkaline and acid types.
 Fig. 23  Schematic representation of the DGFC types: (a) alkaline direct glycerol fuel cell (AEM-DGFC), (b) acid direct glycerol fuel cell (PEM-DGFC) and (c) alkaline–acid direct glycerol fuel cell (AA-DGFC). 
Due to the fact that the electro-oxidation rate of glycerol in alkaline media is considerably higher than in acidic media, alkaline-direct glycerol fuel cells have gained more attention from the scientific community.435–437
In this section the progress achieved over the last few years in the design of the electrocatalyst composition for all types of DGFCs will be reviewed, since they are vital components of fuel cell systems and represent a critical feature for the development of high-performance fuel cells.
For instance, there are studies438 that suggest gold and platinum based electrocatalysts as the best materials for glycerol oxidation in alkaline than in acid media, and Pt is less active than Au in alkaline medium (at high overpotentials).
Other studies demonstrated that Pt has the highest activity in the low potential region, with a turnover frequency (TOF) 4–5 times higher than that on Pd and 20–30 times higher than that on Au at −0.3 V vs. Hg/HgO/1.0 M KOH.439
5.2 Alkaline direct glycerol fuel cells (AEM-DGFCs)
Glycerol electro-oxidation reaction is not a simple reaction because it involves the breakage of two C–C bonds and therefore several pathways are possible, thus a deeper understanding of the whole process is highly desirable. For instance, in alkaline medium, during electro-oxidation of glycerol many soluble products are formed (see Scheme 9), such as glyceraldehyde, glyceric acid, glycolate, glycerate, glyoxylic acid, dihydroxyacetone, hydroxypyruvic acid, mesoxalic acid, oxalic acid, tartonate, oxalate and CO32− and several pathways are possible until it is completely oxidized to CO2 through the transfer of 14 electrons. The complete oxidation of glycerol to carbonate, in alkaline medium, is described by eqn (8): C3H8O3 + 20OH− → 3CO32− + 14H2O + 14e−(8)
 Scheme 9  Products formed by glycerol oxidation on electrocatalysts in alkaline medium. Compounds in boxes have been detected in the anode compartments of DAFCs. The compound inside the circle has been exclusively detected in half cells. Reproduced with permission from ref. 321, Copyright 2009 American Chemical Society. 
Re-adsorption and diffusion of the intermediary species have a negative impact on the total efficiency of the system. One way to improve the performance of the cell is to design an electrocatalyst440,441 and a support442,443 more resistant to the poisoning effect induced by the adsorbed intermediates and more selective towards the CO2 formation.440,444 There are only a few studies giving an overview of the advances in AEM-DGFC, in which different parameters affecting the cell performance are discussed,321,434,445 most of them presenting a summary on the electrocatalysts used in direct alcohol fuel cells.321,431,445

5.2.1 Noble metal based electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs. 
Pt based electrocatalysts have been identified as the most efficient electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs because they demonstrated high activity towards the electro-oxidation of alcohols in general,446–448 and of glycerol in particular.437,449,450 Moreover, the higher the Pt percentage, the better the cell performance in the overall current density range, as a result of the enhanced kinetics of the glycerol oxidation and the enhanced mass transportation of the thinner catalytic layer, according to the data presented by Linares et al.450 Also, the fuel cell investigation demonstrated that the Pt/C (40 wt%) anode in AEM can yield a peak power density (PPD) of 124.5 mW cm−2 with 2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M high purity glycerol at 80 °C.437,439 This power current density is similar to the ones obtained in direct methanol (168 mW cm−2) and ethanol (160 mW cm−2) AEMFCs for multi-metallic catalyst systems (3 mgPtRu cm−2 for methanol and 1 mgPd–(Ni–Zn–P) cm−2 for ethanol) under optimized experimental conditions (i.e. fuel composition flow rate, and temperature).374,451 It is important to note that Pt/C did not show a drop in performance when the fuel was switched to the biodiesel derived crude glycerol (88 wt%), and a PPD of 120 mW cm−2 was obtained under similar conditions (2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol) indicating high stability of the Pt/C anode against the contamination/poisoning of the impurities in crude glycerol.439 However, the PPD presented a volcano-type behaviour as a function of crude glycerol concentration, and this can be explained by the fact that the lower glycerol concentration led to lower coverage rate of glycerol on the surface of the catalysts, while the higher crude glycerol will lead to a lower OH− coverage on the catalyst surface, both situations result in a lower performance. The highest performance was obtained when the cell was fed with 6.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol, the Pt/C anode exhibits a very high PPD of 184.2 mW cm−2.439
However, Pt has several drawbacks and besides the poisoning problem, the price and its rare reserves are very important from the economic point of view and thus, developing efficient electrocatalysts free or containing low amounts of Pt is critical for the design of performant AEM-DGFCs. In this line, an unprecedented performance of about 269 mW cm−2 was achieved by using the surface dealloyed PtCo supported on carbon nanotube (CNT) anode catalyst with only 0.5 mgPt cm−2 in a membrane electrode assembly (MEA, with a commercial Fe-based cathode) for a direct crude glycerol (88 wt%)/O2 AEMFC operated at 80 °C and ambient pressure.452 This performance is higher than the one obtained using Pt/CNTs (229 mW cm−2), because of the d-band center of the Pt shift as a result of lattice strain and optimized electronic properties tuned by the sub-layer Co atoms, leading to weakened chemisorption of oxygenated species (see Fig. 24).
 Fig. 24  Schematic illustration of the synthesis of surface dealloyed PtCo supported on CNT catalyst. Reproduced with permission from ref. 452, Copyright 2013 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Recently, another transition metal used in combination in different proportions with Pt is copper. The tests made in fuel cells, directly fed with 2 M glycerol solution at 90 °C, indicated PtCu/C (90:10) as the most effective electrocatalyst in the oxidation reaction of the fuels when compared with Pt/C and Cu/C, due to the synergy between the constituents of the electrocatalyst.453 The recorded PPD for PtCu/C (90:10) is about 18 mW cm−2 while Pt/C and Cu/C showed 6 and 4 mW cm−2, respectively.
Extensive research has been carried out to replace Pt, and compared to Pt-based electrocatalysts, less expensive and widely available Pd and/or Au based electrocatalysts have been exploited as substitutes in AEM-DGFCs due to their outstanding stability in alkaline media. Carbon supported Pd and Au nanoparticle (NP) catalysts with small diameters (∼3.5 nm), prepared through a solution-phase reduction method, showed at 80 °C and 6.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol a PPD of 93.9, and 50.1 mW cm−2 on Pd/C (40 wt%), and Au/C (40 wt%) anodes, respectively.439 As in the case of Pt/C, the concentration of crude glycerol plays an important role, its effect on fuel performances is depicted in Fig. 25b and c. The PPD presented a volcano-type behaviour against the crude glycerol concentration. The highest PPDs were obtained with 3.0 M crude glycerol with Au/C anode catalysts, and 1.0 M crude glycerol with Pd/C catalyst.
 Fig. 25  (a) Polarization and power density curves of Pt/C, Pd/C, and Au/C anode AEM-DGFCs with high purity glycerol fuel at 80 °C; anode fuel: 2 M KOH + 1 M high purity glycerol; (b) effect of crude glycerol concentration on fuel cell performances using Pd/C and (c) Au/C anodes. Adapted with permission from ref. 439, Copyright 2013 Elsevier Publications. 
From Fig. 25b and c we can observe that the crude glycerol fuel yielded lower open circuit voltages (OCVs) and PPDs on Pd/C and Au/C anode AEMFCs, as compared with Pt/C performances. Also, compared to the results obtained with high purity glycerol (in Fig. 25a), fed with 2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol, the OCVs on Pd/C and Au/C anode AEMFCs dropped by 0.08 and 0.16 V, respectively, while the PPD was reduced by 10.7 and 27.2 mW cm−2, respectively. In the same study, the authors demonstrated that the anode electrode potential on Pd/C catalyst is governed by the catalyst surface coverage rate of both crude glycerol and OH−.
If the crude glycerol concentration increases, it will drastically reduce the adsorption of OH− and therefore a small increase in OCV is observed on the Pd/C anode. In the case of Au/C electrocatalyst, there are studies which indicated that the glycerol electro-oxidation will facilitate the formation of highly reactive glycerolate on the gold surface,454 which has, as a consequence, a higher OCV, and therefore, glycerol is expected to be a promising fuel for Au anode catalyst-based AEM-DGFCs.
Indeed, in a parallel study Li et al.454 obtained high performance using as the anode Au/C (30 wt% Au) electrocatalyst in an AEM-DGFC: at 80 °C, the OCV and PPD reached 0.67 V and 57.9 mW cm−2, respectively. However, in addition to the OH− concentration and GLY concentration, the OH− to GLY ratio seems to be as much as important for the high performance of DGFCs.455
Another study highlighted the excellent electrocatalytic activity of Pd supported on MWCNTs in terms of PPD, as high as 80 mW cm−2 in a fuel cell working with glycerol at 80 °C. The authors claim that such a remarkable electrocatalytic activity of Pd/MWCNTs can be associated both with the high dispersion of the metal particles and to the intrinsic properties of the MWCNTs.367
In the last few years, Li's group reported that the combination of two metals, Pd and Ag, with two different functions is more efficient than Pd for alcohol oxidation in general, and glycerol oxidation in particular.359 Thus, direct glycerol fuel cells achieved a PPD of 276.2 mW cm−2, using PdAg/CNTs (0.5 mgPd per MEA−1) with an average particle size of 2.7 nm, as an anode catalyst at 80 °C and ambient pressure.359 This underlines the positive role of Ag being involved in the cleavage of the C–C bond in the conversion of glycerol to oxalate, improving in this way the fuel efficiency of glycerol oxidation, while Pd can facilitate deprotonation of alcohol in a base electrolyte. In continuing this work, Pd, Ag mono, and bimetallic nanoparticles supported on carbon nanotubes (Ag/CNTs, Pd/CNTs, Pd1Ag1/CNTs, and Pd1Ag3/CNTs (20 wt%)) prepared using an aqueous-phase reduction method were used as working catalysts in an AEM-DGFCs.455 In this study, the PPDs are inferior to the one presented above, but also the cell parameters are different, such as different KOH concentrations (4 M vs. 6 M) or different reaction temperatures (60 °C vs. 80 °C). However, here also better results were obtained on the PdAg bimetallic catalyst due to the synergistic effect of promotion of intermediate aldehyde oxidation by Ag and C–C breakage of C3 oxygenates along with a larger amount of Pd0 presented on the PdAg nanoparticle surface.
In the same line, last year, Pd and Ag were deposited on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) and it was shown that the interactions of PdAg NPs with rGO can control the performances through mediating the electronic characteristics of metal NPs and the reactant absorptions.456 With a peak current density of about 10328 A mgPd−1 on Pd0.05Ag0.95/rGO it was possible to overcome the performance achieved by PdAg (1:1) deposited on CNTs (20% metal loading) of 8530 A mgPd−1,359 the outstanding performance resulting from the intrinsic electron characteristics and lattice structure of PdxAgy(m)/rGO.456 Moreover, adding Ag to Pd, besides promoting the cell performance, also lowers the cost of the anode catalyst.
Since the Pd and Ag combination proved to be the most promising constituent for anodes in AEM-DGFCs, in order to further decrease the price of the fuel cell, parallel studies were conducted on PgAg/CNTs using polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) thin films with different pore sizes as potential separators of fuel and oxidant streams.432
The performances of DGFCs with PTFE thin film were compared with an anion-exchange 116 membrane (A901) and the PPD of the DGFC with A901 AEM is 277.7 mW cm−2 which is 22.6% higher than the PPD of the porous PTFE thin film. However, the authors claimed that using a PTFE thin film separator without further synthesis or modification breaks the paradigm and demonstrates that a PTFE thin film separator is capable of operating with lower ionic resistance than benchmark state-of-the-art anion exchange membranes.432
Literature survey indicates other binary or ternary palladium based electrocatalysts, since the addition of a second or a third element may modify the electronic or surface strain effects of catalyst surfaces. Thus, electrocatalysts such as PdAu/C (50:50), PdSn/C (50:50), PdAuSn/C (50:40:10) and PdAuSn/C (50:10:40)431 or Pd87Cu13/C,457 Pt3Sn/C,458 PdAg and PdAu nanoparticles loaded on carbon black,459 Pd–(Ni–Zn)/C460 or using different supports, such as Pd/Ti nanotube arrays were used as anodes in AEM-DGFCs,461 but with lower performances than the one presented and discussed previously in this review.
The effect of electrocatalyst nanostructure and morphology on the cell performances has also been the subject of several studies. For instance, in this year, strain engineering was studied, a new strategy that may open new avenues to highly efficient catalysts for direct alcohol fuel cells and many other applications.462 In this direction, the highly strained Au–Ag–Pd alloy nanowires outperformed the less strained counterparts, showing mass activities of up to 11.1 A mgPd−1 for glycerol oxidation. The authors claimed that these activity values are an order of magnitude higher than those of the commercial Pd/C and higher than those of most Pd-based catalysts reported to date;463,464 suggesting promising applicability of these catalysts in DGFC applications.
Studies employing another interesting nanostructure were reported, for instance multimetallic Pd nanosponges with enhanced electrocatalytic activity for glycerol oxidation.465 The performances of PdAuNi nanosponge were due to the modified electronic states of Pd but also to the typical sponge nanostructures. The electrocatalytic activity was found to follow the order: Pd62Au21Ni17 > Pd64Au4Ni32 > Pd61Au31Ni8 >Pt/C. The peak current values of Pd62Au21Ni17 nanosponges reached 0.047 A cm−2, which was twice as high as that of Pt/C (0.023 A cm−2).


5.2.2 Noble metal-free electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs. 
Noble metal-free electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs as well as their oxides have attracted a lot of attention recently due to their low cost and abundance, yet their performances are below those of their counter-candidates based on noble metals. Thus, many electrocatalysts have been currently studied, especially based on Ni and Cu with good activity and high tolerance to CO poisoning.
For instance, a highly efficient electrocatalyst was developed using nickel nanoparticles supported on nitrogen doped graphene (Ni-NGr)466 that yielded a PPD of 10.4 mA cm−2. This performance is higher than the one obtained on nickel ion implanted modified indium tin oxide (Ni-NP/ITO),467 and nickel nanoparticles stabilized by PVP.468 The authors indicated that the improved performance originated from the very high distribution of Ni nanoparticles on N-graphene which possessed a high surface area, but also to the presence of a synergistic combination of Ni nanoparticles and NGr sheets that induced a fast electron transfer effect due to nitrogen doped graphene.
In a recent study, the influence of the electrodeposited Ni nanoparticle morphology, distribution, structure and performance for energy production from glycerol was investigated.469 The support, commercial 3D-carbon fibers (CFs), was first functionalized with O- and S-like functionalities via electrochemical pretreatment in NaOH and H2SO4 and it was found that the crystallite size of the electrodeposited Ni is reduced from 154 nm for the untreated CF to 45 and 54 nm for the activated CF in H2SO4 and NaOH, respectively. The peak current of the glycerol oxidation exponentially increases with the electrodeposited Ni loading, while Ni modified pretreated CFs exhibited ∼2 fold higher electrocatalytic activity for glycerol electrooxidation at all the studied Ni loadings, suggesting the essential enhancing role of the preactivation step. In order to further enhance the performance of the glycerol electro-oxidation in alkaline medium, macroporous Ni electrodes decorated with Cu particles were reported.470 Indeed, the decoration with Cu was valuable and the electrocatalytic activity was enhanced as confirmed by the negative shift in the onset oxidation potential (ca. 100 mV) together with an increase in oxidation current that is up to 1.5-fold during the cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements, compared to the undecorated Ni foam.
Recently, Co3O4 has attracted great attention, being extensively investigated in electrochemical oxidation reactions due to its rich redox chemistry that results in high electrochemical activity.471,472 In glycerol oxidation, Co3O4 exhibited a current of 115 mV at 1.524 V using a 5 M KOH solution.
Copper oxides were employed as electrocatalysts based on the same arguments as for cobalt oxides, but their performance is seriously affected by the poor inherent conductivity of Cu2O.473 To overcome this issue, Cu nanoparticles were incorporated into Cu2O to enhance the conductivity and as a consequence the electrocatalytic activity of the Cu2O catalysts due to synergistic effects between Cu and Cu2O.474 Further doping with other metals (Ni, Fe, Co) of Cu/Cu2O dendrite foam structures induced high electrocatalytic activity and stability for glycerol electrooxidation.475
Although enormous effort has been devoted to the development of anodes containing non-noble metals for AEM-DGFCs in the last few years, the goal of achieving high performance is still unreachable at this moment.

5.3 Acid direct glycerol fuel cells (PEM-DGFCs)
Due to the smaller ohmic losses as compared to the alkaline electrolysers, the PEM electrolysers represent the best choice for the electrolytic production of hydrogen.476 The advantage of low ohmic losses makes the PEM electrolysers to operate at current densities greater than 1 A cm−2, but require the use of mainly expensive and rare catalysts, such as platinum, ruthenium and iridium.
These appear to be the most stable and active electrode materials in acidic medium and represent the best option for performing the glycerol electro-oxidation process.477 Among them, platinum was considered unavoidable for a long time when talking about the activation and dissociative adsorption of glycerol at the electrode surface478 and the formation of adsorbed species that are further oxidized and leave behind protons. Thus, based on the use of platinum electrode materials, there are several papers dealing with the exploration of the processes arising from glycerol oxidation.444,479 Other mono-metallic480 or multi-metallic catalytic materials481–484 can perform the electro-oxidation of glycerol.
Nevertheless, it should be stated that in the field of PEM-DGFCs most of the studies mainly report phenomena that are more related to the electrocatalytic glycerol oxidation and slightly or even not related to the real operation of the entire fuel cell ensemble.485,486 In the field of electrocatalytic glycerol oxidation, during the last decade, there were several excellent reviews477,487 focusing on describing the progress achieved in this research area.
5.4 Alkaline–acid direct glycerol fuel cells (AA-DGFCs)
Improvement of DGFCs is required as the electrochemistry of these cells is challenging in several ways. As mentioned in the previous section, in AEM-DGFCs, unlike other simple alcohols, the electro-oxidation of glycerol is a very complex process.488 Finally, at the anode, the glycerol is only partially oxidized to CO2 and the process ended up to oxygenated products due to the difficulty to perform two C–C bond cleavages and several parallel reactions. Another challenge is related to the reduction process taking place at the cathode. The Pt-specific PD of the most common Pt electrodes present values higher than 1.1 gPt kW−1, while the targeted value is less than 0.2 gPt kW−1. Such values can be achieved in theory at low Pt loadings (<0.05 mpPt cm−2).489 However, lowering the Pt loadings was not sufficient to reduce the Pt-specific PD, due to the voltage loss. One of the most important contributions to voltage loss is the sluggish O2 reduction kinetics taking place at the cathode, which is a four-electron process with low probability of occurrence (eqn (9)).490 O2 + 4H+ + 4e− → 2H2O(9)
Besides all these aspects, the use of O2 as an oxidant implies the formation of water, which tends to flood the pores of gas diffusion layers and thus slowing down the reactant transport.270 To avoid these issues, hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) was found to be a good alternative as an oxidant to the cathode, with the following advantages: (i) the theoretical voltage of the fuel cell is considerably increased, from 1.23 V to 1.78 V at the cathode in DEFCs (see Fig. 15);270 (ii) H2O2 reduction process implies only the use of 2 electrons, thus offering a low activation barrier and therefore higher efficiency of the fuel cell (eqn (10)); (iii) can operate in an anaerobic environment, which might expand the use of DGFCs under underwater or outer space conditions491 and (iv) no water flooding problem as water is no longer a product derived from the reduction of H2O2.492 Moreover, H2O2 reduction in alkaline media has a theoretical voltage of 0.87 V, while under acidic conditions its reduction potential increased up to 1.78 V (see Fig. 15), making H2O2 a suitable oxidizer in acidic solutions. H2O2 + 2e− → 2OH−(10)
As presented in Fig. 15, combining the benefits of alkaline direct ethanol fuel cells with those of acid direct ethanol fuel cells (low activation of reduction reaction of H2O2 under acidic conditions), a new class with higher theoretical voltage values results (2.52 V). Therefore, extrapolating the data already existing for DEFCs, a new class of hybrid fuel cells using glycerol was born with the same goal of avoiding the drawbacks experienced with alkaline direct glycerol fuel cells and acid direct glycerol fuel cells. The expected theoretical voltage is 2.6 V.493
The structure of the hybrid fuel cell demands the use of: (i) an anode in an alkaline environment (usually sodium hydroxide) with a lower potential, (ii) a cationic exchange membrane (CEM) which allows the transfer of Na+ ions through the cathode, and (iii) a higher cathode potential in an acid environment (usually sulphuric acid). The schematic of such an alkaline–acid fuel cell using H2O2 as the oxidant is presented in Fig. 23b.
Thus, the alkaline environment at the anode appears when sodium hydroxide (NaOH) is added to the glycerol solution (anolyte), so that hydroxyl ions are formed (see eqn (11)) which further migrate through the CEM to the cathode. At the same time, the glycerol oxidation reaction (GOR) will take place (see eqn (12)). 14NaOH → 14Na+ + 14OH−(11) C3H8O3 + 14OH− → 3CO2 + 11H2O + 14e−(12)
The acid environment at the cathode side, however, appears when sulphuric acid (H2SO4) is added and forms protons and ionized SO42− (see eqn (13)). The ionized SO42− species will further interact with the Na+ ions coming from the anode with the formation of sodium sulphate (Na2SO4) at the cathode side. In the meantime, the formed protons at the cathode and the electrons coming from the anode will interact with the oxidizer (H2O2) and water is formed in the catholyte (see eqn (14)). 7H2SO4 → 14H+ + 7SO42−(13) 7H2O2 + 14H+ + 14e− → 14H2O(14)
Finally, the overall reaction of this alkaline–acid direct glycerol fuel cell, with the theoretical voltage of 2.6 V, can be given by the following eqn (15): C3H8O3 + 14NaOH + 7H2SO4 + 7H2O2 → 3CO2 + 25H2O + 7Na2SO4(15)
Taking into account that alkaline–acid direct glycerol fuel cells possess all the advantages of a good anode and cathode working in alkaline and acid media, respectively, the challenge in using these hybrid fuel cells is provided by the working parameter conditions. Therefore, in the following we will discuss in detail the issues experienced in AA-DGFCs and the best operational parameters found for the alkaline–acid direct glycerol fuel cells.

5.4.1 Operation conditions at the anode. 
Flow rate, glycerol concentration and variation of glycerol to sodium hydroxide ratio (G:NaOH) are usually evaluated to understand the influence of fuel flow and concentration. The flow rate of the fuel stream has very little impact on PD when the initial flow rate of 1 mL min−1 was increased to 2 mL min−1.494 This result was also confirmed by Sangkheaw et al.495 who found no significant difference in average current density for anolyte flow rates ranging from 1 mL min−1 to 2 mL min−1. However, decreasing the anolyte flow rate below 1 mL min−1 will result in a significant drop of current density.
Regarding the concentration, at a high G:NaOH ratio of 2, a low power density (PD) of 50 mW cm−2 was obtained, while a decrease of the ratio to 1 and further to 0.5 resulted in an increase of PD to 80 mW cm−2 and 105 mW cm−2, respectively.494 It can be concluded that the electrolyte concentration highly influences the overall fuel cell performance; for a constant NaOH concentration there is no reason to increase the glycerol concentration since this will determine a decrease of the PD. In this particular case, the pH ranges from 14.3 to 13.7 when the concentration of H2O2 increases. Such behaviour can be justified by the pH difference, which is directly related to the G:NaOH ratio. It was shown that a high pH value at the anode, when alcohols are used as the fuel, is very important for achieving a good cell performance.348

Table 13 presents the rate of oxidation of alcohols used in direct fuel cells and their dependency on the pH used. When the pH increases from 12 to 14 the oxidation rate increases by 1–2 orders of magnitude for each alcohol.
Table 13 The oxidation rate of each alcohol in the electrochemical cell reported after 5 min at 0.43 V vs. RHE in the pH range of 12 to 14 in 1 M fuel + appropriate concentration of [OH−]348
