Therefore, the oxidation rate of alcohols increases with the pH and is the main reason why direct alcohol fuel cells work well when a high concentration of NaOH is found in the fuel stream. However, Sangkheaw et al.,495 who also studied the influence of the G:NaOH ratio on the cell performance, noticed that a high concentration of NaOH results in an increase in viscosity, which consequently leads to mass transport issues at the cathode interface.
Thus, a compromise between a low G:NaOH ratio and increased viscosity must be found in order to obtain the optimum cell performance. Another alternative is to improve the microporous layer (MPL) at the anode so the mass transfer loss can be improved. Banjong et al.493 investigated the effect of MPL at the anode, using a hydrophilic and a hydrophobic MPL. This study suggests that the use of a hydrophobic MPL induces less fluctuation in the current density of the cell compared with the hydrophilic MPL or no MPL cells. The cell polarization indicates that the no-MPL cells outperform, however the presence of the hydrophobic MPL induces a satisfactorily high and stable current density of 432.57 mA cm−2 with the standard deviation as low as 22.97 mA cm−2.


5.4.2 Operation conditions at the cathode. 
One of the most important chemical reaction aspects that deserve great attention is H2O2 transformation, at high potentials, that can occur at the cathode. Hydrogen peroxide is unstable at high potentials and besides the reduction reaction, undesired reactions such as decomposition can occur with release of oxygen, protons and electrons (see eqn (16)):496 H2O2 → O2 +2H+ + 2e−(16)
Major issues can appear if the produced oxygen performs a competing reduction reaction with water formation (eqn (9)), which develops a much lower potential (1.23 V) than the H2O2 reduction reaction (1.78 V), leading to, as a consequence, a low voltage fuel cell.497
Besides the disadvantage of oxygen reduction at low potentials, the presence of O2 as a gas at the cathode interface can be an impediment in the H2O2 accessibility to the surface, resulting in a reduced performance of the AA-DGFC.493 Therefore, the variation of operating conditions at the cathode can be an alternative to avoid the oxygen formation/reduction at the interface.
Oxidant flowrate. 
Variation of hydrogen peroxide flowrate has been seen as a solution to avoid the formation of a higher amount of oxygen during the undesired decomposition of the oxidant. Banjong et al.493 observed a constant current density of ∼260 mA cm−2 at 0.4 V, when applying 1, 3 and 6 mL min−1 flow rates of H2O2 at the cathode (20 wt% Pt/C). However, intermittent drops along the discharge period were observed when a slow flowrate was used (1 mL min−1) with a standard deviation of discharged current density of 40.33 mA cm−2. Increasing the flow rates to 3 and 6 mL min−1 diminished the standard deviation to 21.48 and 19.32 mA cm−2 (see Fig. 26). Thus, a low flowrate was associated with a deficient transport of the oxidant to the cathode surface. Cell polarization also confirmed that higher flow rates increase the cell performance. The optimized flow rate was found to be 3 mL min−1, since a lower flow rate resulted in unwanted drop of the current (as observed by chronoamperometry, Fig. 26), while at a higher flow rate, despite the high performance, the H2O2 utilization was too low.
 Fig. 26  Chronoamperometry of the AA-DGFC at different oxidant flow rates. H2O2:H2SO4 ratio of 1, working temperature of 80 °C, fuel: 1 M glycerol and 5 M NaOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 493, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications. 
Chino et al.494 observed an increase in power density when flow rates increased, when Pt/C and C black nanoparticles were used as cathodes. The working temperature of the cell was 60 °C with 1 M glycerol as the fuel and 2 M NaOH. The increase of oxidant stream from 1 mL min−1 to 2 mL min−1 yielded a significant increase of power density from 68 mW cm−2 to 105 mW cm−2, when Pt-free C black nanoparticles were used. Using a Pt/C cathode, a much higher power density was observed for a 1 mL min−1 flow rate (214 mW cm−2), which increased to 253 mW cm−2 for a 2 mL min−1 flow rate. Even a lower power density was obtained when a Pt-free cathode was used, the results obtained are not negligible taking into account the cost consideration.
Oxidant concentration. 
Based on eqn (13) and (14), the H2O2:H2SO4 molar ratio should be 1.0, which was why Banjong et al.493 decided to keep the molar ratio constant at this value and varied the concentration of H2O2 (0.1, 0.3, 0.5 and 1.0 mol L−1).
As can be observed in Fig. 27, the chronoamperograms show that the average current densities increase with the increase of H2O2 concentration as follows: for 0.1 mol L−1 H2O2 67.15 mA cm−2 current density was obtained, while an average current density of 239.67 mA cm−2 was observed for 10 times higher H2O2 concentration. The cell polarization tests confirmed the results obtained by chronoamperometry. However, an abrupt drop in cell voltage was observed near 60 mA cm−2 due to mass transfer limitation.
 Fig. 27  Chronoamperometry of AA-DGFC at different H2O2:H2SO4 ratios and a fixed oxidant flow rate of 3 mL min−1. Working temperature of 80 °C, fuel: 1 M glycerol and 5 M NaOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 493, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications. 


5.4.3 Other parameters. 
AA-DGFCs were investigated to determine the best working temperature for this class of fuel cells. Chronoamperometry and cell polarization confirmed the same conclusion that increasing the temperature from 30 to 80 °C will result in higher current density. Such behaviour can be rationalised by accepting that the high cell performance at high temperature is related to the high OCV, which is directly dependent on the temperature according to Nernst's equation.
The highest jump was observed when the temperature was increased from 70 to 80 °C. Such a rise of current density can be explained by the glycerol electro-oxidation kinetics in alkaline medium, which is enhanced at higher temperature. Despite the remarkable current density observed, higher fluctuations in magnitude were registered at 80 °C, therefore 70 °C was chosen as the best working temperature for the AA-DGFC.
The Nafion® ionomer content was found to increase the hydrophilicity of the anode when a hydrophilic MPL is used. Increasing the hydrophilicity will decrease the mass transport loss due to the high viscosity of the glycerol–NaOH anolyte. An optimum content of 20% Nafion® in MPL yielded a very good power density of the cell, 329 mW cm−2. A higher content of Nafion® will adsorb onto the MPL surface reducing its porosity, inhibiting the mass transport of the anolyte and deteriorating the cell performance.

6. New electrode configurations for portable hydrogen micro fuel cells
6.1 Short overview
Portable hydrogen fuel cells have received renewed attention in recent years driven by the progress made in fuel cells and hydrogen storage options.498 Compared with other portable fuel cells, their principal advantages are the easy anode reaction that allows for higher power densities per unit active area, and the cleaner combustion with sole production of water. On the other hand, they require stored hydrogen for operation which compromises the volume and weight energy densities. State-of-the-art hydrogen storage options for portable systems, like those based on metal-hydrides, complex compounds, and adsorbed hydrogen, practically attain around 10 gH2 kg−1 and 100 gH2 dm−3 storage densities that translate to effective power densities of 150 W h kg−1 and 1500 W h dm−3, depending on the storage media and assuming 50% conversion efficiency in the fuel cell. These values are similar to those of modern Li ion batteries.
According to the International Electrotechnical Vocabulary (IEV 485-09-23 (ref. 499)), a portable fuel cell is “a fuel cell not intended to be permanently fastened or otherwise secured in a specific location”. Conventional fuel cell designs of small size may fall in this category, without requiring any change in their configuration. A particular type of portable fuel cell is the micro fuel cell, “wearable or easily carried by hand together with the fuel cell cartridge” (IEV 485-09-21), which can produce electricity while ported in the hand of the user, like a battery. This type requires important changes in the cell design with respect to conventional fuel cells.
This section describes changes in the electrode configurations of a conventional PEMFC to make a portable micro fuel cell. PEMFC is most convenient for hydrogen portable applications due to the fast power on–off, low working temperature, and high-power density. The first part describes essential differences in the configuration and operation of a portable PEMFC with respect to a conventional PEMFC. In the second part, some main features of anodes and cathodes for portable PEMFCs are described.
6.2 Convective and passive PEMFC configurations
Conventional PEMFCs require a convective flow of the reactant gases through the electrodes, consisting of hydrogen and air streams flowing at the back of the anode and cathode, respectively, parallel to the cell plane. The main purpose is to accelerate the transport of reactants towards the catalyst layers and the rejection of the water produced, with other positive effects such as the homogenization of reaction rates and temperature over the active area region and helping the rejection of excess heat from the cell. Gas convection is essential to attain power densities above 1 W cm−2 with respect to the active area in conventional cells.
However, convective operation requires elements that increase the weight, volume, and cost of the stack and fuel cell system, which make it unsuitable for small portable power. The flow of gases is set by grooved plates at the back of the porous electrodes, called flow-field or bipolar plates, which also serve as current collectors and cell separators in a stack. Such plates, preferentially metal or graphite plates, contribute significantly to the weight (>70%) and volume (>80%) of the stack, and about 15% of the cost.500 In addition, auxiliary elements are necessary to maintain the gas flow, like fans, valves, purging and recirculation systems, gas conditioning, and stack thermal control, which further increase the weight, volume, and cost of the fuel cell system, while decreasing the power efficiency due to auxiliary consumption. The auxiliary charges and efficiency losses of the convective PEMFC configuration make it unsuitable when scaling to small portable applications, where the dimensions, weight, autonomy, safety, and operability requirements are different than in larger power systems.498
In the passive PEMFC configuration the fuel cell can generate a constant power without applying convective forces to gases. The anode and cathode operate in contact with static hydrogen and air atmospheres, which avoid the use of convective components and favour fuel cell system portability. The transport of gases and water occurs by passive processes, like capillary diffusion and natural convection caused by gradients in chemical potentials, density, and temperature within the electrodes and around the cell.501–503 Passive conditions require optimising liquid water interaction and transport within the porous electrodes, to sustain continuous current generation. Fig. 28 compares the schemes of a convective and a passive fuel cell.
 Fig. 28  Working principles of (a) the conventional-convective, and (b) the full-passive PEMFC configurations. 
The following parts of Section 6 describe changes implemented in the architecture of the anode and cathode to attain maximal power output in passive operation.
6.3 The passive dead-end anode
Operating in a static atmosphere of hydrogen, without forcing hydrogen flow or purging,504,505 allows for more simple auxiliary systems and the possibility to attain 100% faradaic efficiency, which benefit the portability of the fuel cell system. On the other hand, passive transport of hydrogen and heat requires changes in the anode electrode design to maintain high power production rates. The principal transport limitation in a passive anode is caused by the accumulation of water in the pores, coming from the cathode, which blocks catalyst centres and leads to cell failure.501,505 To avoid anode flooding, some changes in the conventional anode design must be introduced, like those described below. Of course, dry hydrogen feeding will be most convenient for a passive dead-end anode, like that obtained from a metal-hydride canister or from compressed hydrogen storage.

6.3.1 Water permeable window. 
Adding a water permeable window to the anodic chamber for water elimination from the cell was first reported by Karst et al.,506 and incorporated by some of us in functional portable fuel cells.504 The anodic window must be made of a hydrophilic membrane able to absorb and transport water driven by a chemical potential gradient in liquid water and/or vapour (humidity gradient). At the same time, the membrane must be impermeable to hydrogen gas, and have appropriate mechanical strength and chemical stability.
Materials for water permeation membranes are preferentially based on modified polymer matrices with hydrophilic components. Nafion® polymer has been used as an anodic PEMFC window,504 whose structure, combining a hydrophobic fluorocarbon backbone and hydrophilic side polar chains, favours channel formation upon swelling for liquid water conduction with a high self-diffusivity of 10−6 to 10−5 cm2 s−1.507 One drawback of this material is the high interfacial transport resistance across the vapour–Nafion® interface that hinders water vapour permeation, whereas it is negligible for the liquid–membrane interface.507,508 Consequently, anode flooding could occur in the cell before the window starts to conduct water. The use of other materials for anodic water permeation in a PEMFC is a matter of future research, such as those based on hydrophilic polymers like poly(ethylene oxide), PEO-copolymers, or hydrophobic polymers blended with hydrophilic materials.
The water permeable window must be suitably placed in the anodic chamber of the cell, for easy access of water, and probably assisted with hydrophilic paths to accelerate water movement. A cross-sectional scheme of a cell with a permeable water window in the anode chamber is shown in Fig. 29.
 Fig. 29  Cross-sectional scheme of a passive PEMFC cell with a passive dead-end anode with a water permeable window, and air-breathing cathode. Reproduced with permission from ref. 505, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications. 
The permeable water window has some drawbacks. It requires the anode back plane in contact with the ambient environment so it may be appropriate only for planar stacking configurations. In addition, it increases the volume and cost of the cell, so it may not be suitable for large serial stacking.


6.3.2 Hydrophobic anode. 
Another means to prevent dead-end anode flooding in a passive dead-end anode is by setting a hydrophobic catalyst layer that impedes cathodic water passing to the anode gas diffusion layer. A previous study has shown that using hydrophobic catalyst layers changes liquid water transport inside a PEMFC.509 For instance, a superhydrophobic cathode forces liquid water towards the anode in a conventional PEMFC and facilitates a high hydration level in the membrane which is convenient for the convective operation. In the passive PEMFC, a superhydrophobic anode may be more advantageous to prevent water back-diffusion from the cathode, since lower current densities are attained and membrane drying out is less probable due to the high-water content attained in the cathode (see below).
Hydrophobicity in the catalyst layer is achieved by adding a hydrophobic agent like PTFE.510 This procedure is used for the hydrophobization of gas diffusion layers to improve their liquid water transport. Its use in catalyst layers is also reported, though it poses problems due to the blocking of pores and Pt particles that may lead to a decrease in porosity, catalyst active area, and electronic conductivity of the layer. Such deleterious effects may be prevented by adding carbon nanotubes,511 but it increases the complexity of the catalyst layer preparation and operation.
The second way to confer hydrophobic character to the catalyst layer without the need for additives, is by depositing the layer using the electrospray technique, which confers a dendritic morphology with hydrophobic properties (Fig. 30).512,513 Electrospray deposition of catalytic suspensions of the Pt/C particles and the ionomer (Nafion®) has proven to be useful for the hydrophobization of the cathodic catalyst layer of conventional cells.509
 Fig. 30  SEM images of the morphology of a Pt/C catalyst layer prepared by the electrospray method, showing the surface (left) and cross-section with open pore walls (right). Inset: water drop–surface interaction according to the Cassie–Baxter model. 

6.4 The air-breathing cathode
In the passive air-breathing cathode, oxygen, water, and heat are exchanged with the ambient environment by natural forces. Among other properties, the passive cathode works under high saturation conditions in the catalyst and gas diffusion layers,505 with consequent increase in the tortuosity of the oxygen diffusion path which decreases the maximum current generation. At exceedingly high saturation levels, the access of oxygen to the catalyst particles may be available from the dissolved phase, even further limiting the current. To palliate the severe limitations posed by passive operation, conditions for liquid water interaction and passive transport within the pores must be optimised, involving the porous layers of the cathode and the cathodic plate design. Passive water transport also makes the cell response highly dependent on the ambient environment parameters, like humidity, temperature, natural convection, and cell orientation.

6.4.1 Limiting transport. 
The operation of a passive air-breathing cathode is mostly limited by the high liquid water content (saturation), which depends on the porosity of the catalyst and gas diffusion layers and on the hydrophobicity of pore walls. Saturation conditions give rise to two transport regimes for liquid water.514 Under high saturation values, above the irreducible value (s > sw0), funicular saturation connects both faces of the electrode with liquid water domains which activates the capillary transport driven by a difference in water pressure between the cathode–membrane interface and the ambient environment. Below the irreducible saturation, the absence of liquid paths allows water transport by slow evaporation–condensation events between the insular liquid domains. Since passive cathodes normally operate under high saturation, the evacuation of water by capillary transport is active in the steady-state, and the limiting current density (iL,H2O) can be related to a liquid water pressure difference by means of Darcy's law: (17)where F (= 96485 C mol−1) is the Faraday constant, ρw (= 1 kg m−3) the water density, Mw (= 18 g mol−1) the water molar weight, kw (m2) the intrinsic water permeability, μw (Pa s) the viscosity of water, pw (Pa) the water phase pressure, and L the electrode gas diffusion layer thickness (for simplification liquid water transport limitation will be entirely attributed to water content of this layer which is reasonable because it is normally much thicker than the catalyst layer). kw depends on water saturation (kw = k(sw − sw0)/(1 − sw0)m, where k is the permeability of the porous phase, sw0 the irreducible water saturation, and m = 4.5 for the gas diffusion layer of fuel cell electrodes). The water pressure gradient is related to the water saturation gradient in the porous electrode by the capillary pressure (pc): (18)where pg (Pa) is the gas phase pressure, σ (N m−1) the surface tension, θi the internal contact angle, ε the layer porosity, and J (s) the Leverett function.509 The gas pressure (pg) is considered constant in the porous media and equal to the ambient air pressure. Saturation of the porous layers also limits oxygen access to the catalyst layer, leading to a limiting current given by the equation: (19)where c* is the oxygen concentration in the ambient environment and Deff is the effective diffusivity of the porous layer, which for a gas diffusion layer is given by:515 Deff = Dbε3.5(1 − s)2.15(20)where Db is the open space, outside the porous layer, diffusivity.

Eqn (17) and (19) describe the two possible current limitations by the gas diffusion layer, by oxygen and by water, as a function of water saturation. Both expressions are compared in Fig. 31 for values of standard PEMFC electrodes, showing that limiting conditions change from water transport to oxygen transport at about s = 0.5, where a maximum limiting current can be attained under passive conditions of 1.5 A cm−2. Experimental values of limiting currents normally fall below 0.5 A cm−2, reflecting the passive air-breathing cathode operating under oxygen transport limitation due to high pore saturation, s > 0.7. The optimal saturation condition is around s = 0.4–0.6, which improves oxygen diffusivity while maintaining sufficient water content for electrochemical reactions and capillary transport. Such values require the preparation of porous layers with an optimised porous structure and hydrophobicity, applying to the cathode the same methods described in 6.3.2 for anode hydrophobization.
 Fig. 31  Limiting currents at a passive air-breathing cathode as a function of water saturation, according to the expressions in eqn (17) and (19). Parameter values: k = 3 × 10−8 cm2, μw = 10−3 Pa s, L = 400 μm, σ = 72 × 10−5 N cm−1, θi = 150°, ε = 0.8, c* = 8.5 × 10−5 mol cm−3, Db = 0.20 cm2 s−1, T = 295 K, p = 101325 Pa, RH = 0.3, J (s) as in ref. 516. 


6.4.2 The cathodic plate. 
The cathodic plate of a passive air breathing cell provides compression by clamping pressure to decrease contact resistance between the electrode and the current collector. At the same time, it allows the exchange of air, water, and heat with the ambient environment. Many designs are proposed based on planar rigid plates perforated with holes, as shown in Fig. 32a, where parameters like hole shape and size, open area ratio, plate material, and thickness, must be optimized to improve mass transport and heat transport, and decrease the contact resistance.517 Cell water removal and emission from the plate to the ambient environment must be optimised via natural forces (evaporation, natural convection, or gravity).
 Fig. 32  Images of passive air breathing cells with (a) cathodic planar, and (b) columnar plates. Reproduced with permission from ref. 518 and 505, respectively, Copyright 2009 and 2020, respectively, Elsevier Publications. 
A columnar plate has recently been used to maximize water removal rate and air-breathing performance (see Fig. 32b).505 It allows for a higher mobility of water droplets over the cathode surface which facilitates their removal. The columnar plate makes the cell response dependent on orientation, as shown in Fig. 33.
 Fig. 33  Comparing fuel cell performances with a columnar passive air-breathing cathode in three orientations. Reproduced with permission from ref. 505, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications. 
The cathodic plate also has a significant effect on heat dissipation from the air-breathing cell. Heat generated by entropic and Joule heating may make the temperature of the cell too high for PEMFC operation. Dissipation by natural forces depends on the cell material, plate design, ambient conditions, and orientation. A reduced heat transfer occurs with the cathode facing downwards,519 and may limit cell response by membrane drying.520 Passive air breathing must operate in a delicate equilibrium between membrane drying and electrode flooding which rely on the optimal design of the cell and packaging.521

6.5 Summary
Characteristics of electrodes and cell designs are described for the adaptation of a hydrogen PEMFC to a portable micro fuel cell. The portability is favoured by changing from a convective flow of reactants to passive feeding. Passive feeding poses significant limitations that require making necessary changes in electrode and cell configurations to allow for a continuous operation with maximal power delivery. The passive anode should work under full dead-end conditions, without purging, to attain 100% faradaic efficiency. Avoiding the flooding of the anode is essential for a continuous operation. The passive air-breathing cathode requires optimising the action of natural forces for the exchange of water and oxygen with the ambient environment. Gross power attainable with a passive PEMFC is typically lower than that with a conventional-convective PEMFC, but the overall net system power and power densities are adequate for portable applications with similar performance to modern batteries.
7. Concluding remarks
In the group of the emerging, clean energy technologies comprising the solar energy, geothermal energy, wind energy, and fusion power technology, fuel cells are in a pole position with regard to the effective and practical way of conversion of hydrogen into electricity.
This review article describes and discusses the state-of-the-art of the electrocatalysts used in the last few years in different fuel cells. It is important to note that the relationship between physico-chemical properties and cell performances is decisive for the design of efficient electrocatalysts.
Various catalysts with unique structures and morphologies, such as nanowires, nanosheets, and assembly structures, have been manufactured in recent years, and some of them show excellent catalytic performance characteristics. It is evident that the electrocatalysts must meet several stringent criteria to achieve reliable, high and constant performances, such as (1) high porosity adequate for alcohol and oxygen transport, (2) sufficient electronic conductivity; (3) chemical stability and high C and CO tolerance (assured by well-defined chemical states, which do not suffer major changes) in a reducing fuel atmosphere; (4) compatibility with other DAFC components; (5) ease of fabrication; (6) relatively low cost induced by avoiding the use of Pt or using an ultra-low Pt amount.
Improving the portability of hydrogen PEMFC and DAFC may require new electrode configurations as described in Section 6. In particular, the structures facilitating the passive operation of the anode and cathode are most convenient to alleviate weight and volume, and to increase the net power production of the systems by avoiding auxiliary components required in the conventional-convective fuel cells.
The results discussed in this paper show that fuel cells are promising power sources for portable applications for the near future. Most appropriate are low temperature fuel cell types fed with hydrogen (Section 2), methanol (Section 3), ethanol (Section 4), or glycerol (Section 5). Hydrogen fuel cells provide high power density (>1000 mW cm−2) in conventional cell configurations, but a decrease is expected in real portable operation, under passive feeding conditions. These cells rely on portable hydrogen storage options which limit the energy density. Liquid fuels allow for higher energy density, and promising power densities have been attained in laboratory cells, above 200 mW cm−2 for methanol, and 100 mW cm−2 for ethanol and glycerol. Future improvements should concentrate on optimized electrode and fuel cell designs for reliable and high performance operation under portable power conditions.

Table Content:
Ştefan Neaţu, Florentina Neaţu, Iuliana M. Chirica and Mihaela Florea	Research team, part of the group Catalytic Material and Catalysis, at National Institute of Materials Physics, Romania (NIMP) (https://infim.ro). From left to right: Dr Stefan Neatu (senior researcher), Dr Mihaela Florea (senior researcher), Iulia M. Chirica (PhD student) and Dr Florentina Neatu (senior researcher). The research of the NIMP team is focused on the preparation, characterization and study of the physical properties of new materials connected to high technology products and devices. Their main research areas deal with heterogeneous catalysis, fuel cells and electrochemistry, materials surface science and energy conversion.
Irina Borbáth, Emília Tálas and András Tompos	Research team, part of the Renewable Energy Research Group (RERG), at Research Centre for Natural Sciences, Institute of Materials and Environmental Chemistry (IMEC), Eötvös Loránd Research Network (ELKH), Hungary. From left to right: Dr Irina Borbáth (senior researcher), Dr Emília Tálas (senior researcher) and Dr András Tompos (director of the IMEC and head of the RERG). Researchers of the team have over 20 years of experience in the field of design and preparation of multimetallic supported Pt-based alloy-type catalysts. The special knowledge and research infrastructure of the group allows applying combinatory and high throughput techniques for efficient work. In the last decade the research group emerged as one of the leading Hungarian experts of development and testing of electrocatalysts for PEM fuel cells.
Simona Somacescu and Petre Osiceanu	Dr Petre Osiceanu and Dr Simona Somacescu are senior researchers at the “Ilie Murgulescu” Institute of Physical Chemistry, Bucharest, Romanian Academy. Their main fields of interest are as follows: synthesis protocols for ordered mesoporous materials with nanocrystalline frameworks for SOFCs, PEMFCs and gas sensor applications as well as surfaces, interfaces and thin film characterization on a wide range of materials: nanomaterials, catalysts, sensitive layers for toxic gas detection, biomaterials, alloys, etc. They acquired advanced expertise in surface chemistry assessment related to high-performance, automated, angle resolved and depth profiling for thin and ultrathin layer analysis by point, line and area chemical state XPS. Practical surface analysis is performed for customers from industries such as nanocomposites, pharmaceuticals, sensors, stainless steel, etc.http://www.icf.ro/groups/xps/index.html.
M. Antonia Folgado and Antonio M. Chaparro	M. Antonia Folgado and Antonio M. Chaparro are researchers of the Low Temperature Fuel Cells Group of CIEMAT (Madrid, Spain). The activities of the group comprise research on new materials and electrode processes, characterization techniques, fabrication of components for fuel cells, new fuel cell prototypes, and development of applications with fuel cells. Recently the group has patented a passive fuel cell based on the proton exchange membrane for small and portable applications and it is fed with dry hydrogen and static ambient air (http://rdgroups.ciemat.es/web/pilascomb/pemfc).
